APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        The proposed Project, the Bank's Second Agricultural Credit Project,
covering all of Yugoslavia aims to raise productivity and incomes in the in-
dividual farmer sector, increase utilization or processing capacities in the
social sector, and contribute to institution building in the participating
banks (PBs) by improving their project preparation, appraisal and administra-
tion techniques.
ii.       Although agriculture's role has been declining relatively with
rapid industrialization, it still remains an important sector in Yugoslavia's
economic development. Almost 35% of the population still lives in rural
areas. Agriculture accounts for 16% of the social product, constitutes
roughly 8% of total commodity exports, with livestock, meat, fruit and vege-
table accounting for about two thirds of agricultural exports, and employs
about 42% of the labor force. The social product in agriculture increased
at an average rate of 2.7% per year during 1971-75, with the social sector
output increasing annually at 5.6%, and the individual farmer sector at 1.9%.
Agricultural production fluctuates heavily with weather conditions, and its
growth has not kept pace with consumption growth. The major constraints to
further agricultural development in the social sector are the lack of capital
to develop the scarce available land and to expand and modernize its outdated
processing facilities. The individual farmer sector's main constraints are
lack of capital and credit facilities for on-farm mechanization and purchase
of better quality breeds and fertilizers, and inadequate extension services
for farmers not participating in cooperative arrangements with the social
sector. The Agricultural Development Plan in recognizing this gives priority
to large investments in the problem areas and emphasizes cooperation between
the social and individual farmer sectors. The proposed project is part of the
Plan's development program. It is in line with the Bank's lending strategy
for the agricultural sector in aiming at production growth, and support
for individual farmers.
iii.      The Project would provide funds during the three year period to
finance investments in fruit and vegetable growing, and livestock development
in the private sector, and cold storage, grain drying facilities, fruit and
vegetable processing plants, dairy and milk collection facilities, meat and
broiler processing plants in the social sector.
iv.       The Project would cost Din 4.1 billion (US$231.0 million) of which
32% or Din 1.3 billion (US$75.0 million) would be foreign exchange and would
be financed by the Bank loan. Vojvodjanska Banka (VB), the Borrower, and
the other PBs would finance Din 1.9 billion (US$106.0 million), and the
Project beneficiaries Din 0.9 billion (US$50.0 million) of the total Project
costs.



- ii -
v.        VB would channel the loan proceeds to a PB in each Republic and
Province at the Bank prevailing rate at Board presentation, with a repayment
period of 15 years, including 3 years of grace. VB would receive a commission
of 1% on disbursements. VB would implement directly the Project investment in
Vojvodina. The individual farmer subborrowers as well as social sector enter-
prises would benefit from subloans up to ten years. The interest rate on Bank
loan proceeds would be at 11% while the rates on local funds would be not less
than 5% and 6% for individual farmers and social sector enterprises respectively.
vi.       Procurement would be done by the subborrowers under the supervision
of the VB and the PBs. Procurement of items for fruit and vegetable produc-
tion, livestock and land development, as well as minor civil works and equip-
ment for processing facilities involving contracts not exceeding an amount
equivalent to US$100,000, would be made through existing commercial channels,
or through local bidding procedures already determined to be satisfactory to
the Bank. All other items related mainly to processing facilities and involv-
ing contracts in excess of US$100,000, equivalent, would be procured through
international competitive bidding according to Bank guidelines.
vii.      The Project would contribute to the production target outlined in
the Plan for 1976-1980. While the Project's incremental production would
largely be marketed domestically, total net foreign exchange earnings or
savings over the project's 20 year lifetime would amount to about US$400
million. The Project would generate about 10,200 new permanent employment
opportunities and reduce underemployment for about 16,800 farmers. An addi-
tional 40,000 farmers are expected to benefit from access to agroindustrial
facilities constructed under the Project. About 60% of Project beneficiaries
will include farmers in the rural target group. The overall economic rate
of return of the Project investments, including administrative and training
expenses would be about 22%.
viii.     With the indicated agreements in Chapter VIII of the Report, the
Project would be suitable for a Bank loan of US$75.0 million, for a term
of 15 years, including a grace period of 3 years.



APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of the Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia has
requested a Bank loan for financing a second agricultural credit project in-
cluding investments in fruit and vegetable growing and livestock development
in the individual farmer sector and cold storages, grain drying facilities,
fruit and vegetable processing plans, dairy and milk collection facilities and
meat and broiler processing plants in the social sector. The Project would
include all republics and provinces of Yugoslavia. Its objectives, which
are in line with the federal Social Plan for 1976-1980 and with the Agricul-
tural Development Plan, aim to raise productivity and incomes in the individ-
ual farmer sector, increase utilization or processing capacities in the social
sector, and contribute to institution building in the participating banks by
improving their project preparation, appraisal and administrative techniques.
The Project places priority on lending to the less developed regions of
Yugoslavia, in according them 50% of Project financing, concentrates its
resources for the assistance of small individual farmers and integrates
production programs of individual farmer and social sectors by financing
agroindustries servicing primarily individual farmer sector production.
1.02      The Project would represent the sixth Bank loan for agriculture and
agroindustries in Yugoslavia, following the first Agroindustries Project in
Macedonia (894 YU) in 1973, the first Agricultural Credit Project (1128 YU)
in 1975, the Metohija Multipurpose Project (1360 YU) and the two Agriculture
and Agroindustries Projects in Macedonia (1371 YU) and Montenegro (1370 YU)
in 1976. The proposed Project was identified in August 1976 by a World Bank
mission, and was subsequently prepared by Vojvodjanska Banka with the assis-
tance of the World Bank, the Federal Committee for Agriculture, other banks
participating in the First Agricultural Credit Project and the Agricultural
Secretariats in the respective Republics and Provinces.
1.03      This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission in
November/December 1976, consisting of Messrs. Merghoub, Von Pischke, von
Pogrell, von Klaudy, Wolffelt (Bank) and Mr. Simeon (FAO/IBRD CP).
II. THE AGRICULTURAL SECTOR
2.01      Agriculture in the Economy.  In Yugoslavia almost 35% of the popula-
tion still lives in rural areas. Agriculture accounts for 16% of the social
product, constitutes roughly 8% of total commodity exports and employs about
42% of the labor force of Yugoslavia. Though its role has been declining
relatively with rapid industrialization, it still remains a sector of basic
importance in the overall development of the economy. The social product in
agriculture increased at an average rate of 2.7% per year during 1971-75, with



- 2 -
the social sector output increasing at about 5.6% per year and the private
sector at about 1.8% per year. Average per capita income in agriculture
amounts to about 41% of that in the rest of the economy in 1975.
2.02      Organization of the Agricultural Sector.  One of the important
characteristic features of Yugoslav agriculture is the separate existence of
two sectors i.e. social and individual with different farm structures. The
social sector has its origin in the postwar collectivization policy, and
received further impetus by the Agrarian Reform of 1953, which limited the
size of private or individual holding to 10 ha of cultivable land or 25 ha
of forest land, and brought 276,000 ha of additional land under the social
sector. Since then, the land resources of the social sector have almost
doubled due to reclamation, purchase and lease from private holders. The
social sector in 1975 held 15% of the cultivated land, employed 5% of total
agricultural manpower, produced 26% of the agricultural product and 46% of the
market production. In 1975 there were 2,363 social sector holdings averaging
1,063 ha of cultivated area per holding. The social sector absorbed more than
two third of the gross fixed investment in agriculture in the post war period.
The capital intensity of the investments increased from Din 1,136 per ha (1972
prices) in 1966 to over Din 4,000 per ha in 1975 while the number of trained
workers increased by 56% and the number of permanent workers decreased by 34%.
At the same time the volume of production in the sector increased by about 50%.
The main constraint to further development of the social sector agriculture is
the lack of capital to develop the scarce available land and to expand and
modernize its outdated processing facilities.
2.03      The individual farmer sector comprises about 2.6 million small
peasant holdings of an average size of 4.0 ha. It has about 94% of total
agricultural population and 85% of cultivated land. The productivity in the
individual farmer sector is significantly lower than in the social sector.
Incomes of social sector farm workers average several times those of individ-
ual farmers, the social product per worker at 1975 prices in the individual
farmer sector is only 24% of the same social product in the social sector,
and investments per ha in the private sector is only 11.7% of the investment
per ha in the social sector in 1973. The main reasons for these differences
are (a) lack of capital, (b) lack of credit facilities for the purchase of
better quality seeds and breeds, fertilizers and machinery, (c) lack of exten-
sion services, and (d) overall lower quality of land. About one-third of the
rural population is estimated to be in the relative poverty target group with
incomes per family member of less than Din 6,138 (US$341) per year (para 7.10).
2.04      The individual farmer and social sectors are for the most part com-
plementary rather than competitive. Labor intensive crops, such as vegetables
and tobacco, are almost exclusively produced by individual farmers, who in
addition own 97% of the sheep and 95% of the cattle herds. Cooperation between
the sectors takes the form of trade in input and output, provision of services
by the social sector such as supply of fertilizers, harvesting, cultivation,
marketing and credit and the contracting of joint production. The social
sector also provides most of the extension service available in Yugoslavia.
Government intends to expand cooperation among farmers and between farmers and
social sector organizations to increase productivity in the individual farmer



sector. Such cooperation would enhance modernization of small farms and
mobilize more resources for investments in that sector. The Republics and
Provinces have agreed to support cooperative arrangements through provision
of financing, and interest rate and tax policies. They also agreed to pass
legislation that would allow cooperating farmers to join the health and social
insurance pension schemes of social sector organizations.
2.05      Marketing, Prices and Taxes.  In Yugoslavia there are "official"
social markets for all agricultural products with authorized purchases and
sales, and "informal" markets based on peasant market places for other products.
The value of authorized purchases amounts to 40-50% of total agricultural
product at present; the rest is either consumed by the agricultural population
or sold on peasant markets. There is large regional variation in the relative
importance of authorized market production. Likewise, in the case of indus-
trial crops such as tobacco, sugarbeet, sunflower, total authorized marketed
production is more than 70% of total production while in the case of vegetables
it is only 10%.
2.06      Three types of pricing arrangements are used in Yugoslavia according
to the technical difficulties in the implementation of price policy on various
markets.   These are:
(a) Guaranteed minimum prices by Government applied for all important
agricultural goods (cereals, meat, wool) for which the implementa-
tion of a price guarantee is technically possible;
(b) Minimum prices - without guarantee of the Government - prescribed
mainly for those agricultural goods which need processing such as
milk, sugarbeet and other industrial crops; and
(c) Prices free from intervention, for goods which are marketed mainly
on peasant markets or are highly perishable, so that effective
price control is practically impossible.
2.07      Intervention through official pricing has not greatly affected price
distortions in produce markets because of its limited scope. In addition to
this pricing system, the Government, through federal or local budgets provides
premiums to the producers of basic commodities. These bonuses of 4-5% of the
minimum prices, are in the case of the social sector reserved to finance invest-
ment programs. Taxes on agriculture are limited to income and turnover taxes
in the social sector, and land tax, according to the cadastral value of the
land, in the individual farmer sector. The income tax is generally calculated
proportionally to the income earned. Its rates differ from republic to
republic (0.8% to 13%), and are in Serbia and Kosovo progressive to the level
of income. The land tax rates vary between 20% in Bosnia-Herzegovina and 9.5%
in Croatia of the estimated annual income earned from the land.
2.08      Policy Making in Agriculture.  At the federal level, coordination of
agricultural policies is achieved by the Federal Chamber of Economy and the
Federal Committee for Agriculture. Responsibility for agricultural policy
implementation lies with the Republic Secretariats for Agriculture and is
coordinated at the Federal level. While these institutions set the framework
and kombinats and cooperatives design and implement the actual production



- 4 -
plans. The kombinats and the cooperatives are an effective medium for policy
implementation regarding production and investments, since they have a virtual
monopoly of technicians and management skills.
2.09      Development Strategy and Priorities.  The overall performance of
the agricultural sector in the Plan period 1971-1975 was better than under the
previous plan. Actual growth of total production was 2.7% on average compared
to the projected 3.5%, and the 2.1% realized under the previous social plan.
The individual farmer sector in particular lagged behind - despite its pro-
ductivity gains - realizing only 1.8% annual growth compared to 3.0% in the
Plan. This trend is the result of various aspects of investment, production
and distribution patterns which favor the social sector (paras 2.02-2.03).
In 1973 as a result of Government discussions on agriculture development the
Republics and Autonomous Provinces approved a common Agreement on the Long-
Term Development Policy for Agriculture (Agricultural Development Plan). This
Agreement reflects the realization among political and economic authorities
that increasing food production requires the cooperation of small farmers.
The Agricultural Development Plan's basic objective is to raise production
to meet expected domestic needs and allow for some exports. It emphasizes:
(a) further development of the social sector through modernization of produc-
tion techniques, expansion of processing facilities, better utilization of
available resources and the adjustment of production to changes in demand; and
(b) development of individual farms primarily through cooperation with the
social sector. The Plan's objectives, revised in 1976, are now 4% per annum
agricultural production growth for the period 1976-1980, with the social sector
expected to grow at 8% and the individual farmer sector at 3%. Within the
field of livestock production, the Plan gives high priority to cattle breeding
in both the individual and social sectors. The production of beef is largely
intended for exports, and, accordingly, quality improvement is emphasized;
milk and milk products are expected to be consumed domestically. The Plan
stresses the need for technical and organizational improvements in the collec-
tion and processing of milk, particularly from the private sector. Emphasis
is also given to increased sheep production in mountainous and generally poor
areas. The production of wheat and lye is projected to develop in line with
domestic need, including the formation and maintenance of an adequate level of
reserves. Production of feed grains, particularly corn, is planned to increase
to meet the growing needs of dairy and meat production. Concommitantly, a
significant expansion in storage and drying facilities will become necessary.
The production of vegetables, fruits and grapes is planned to increase in line
with rising living standards at home and increasing export possibilities. The
Mediterranean climate zone, where winter and early spring vegetables can be
grown, should provide the bulk for exports.
2.10      The Plan considers the development of efficient food processing
industries essential to agricultural development and the improvement of product
quality. With rising standards of living domestic demand is expected to shift
increasingly to higher quality and processed products.
2.11      To achieve its objectives the Plan focusses on price policies,
investment policies and cooperation between the social and individual farmer
sectors. The Government intends to continue through interventionist sales
and purchases to keep market prices within 10-15% of minimum prices as agreed



- 5 -
between the producers and the marketing enterprises. It intends also to
develop the system of premiums for basic commodities (cereals, milk, indus-
trial crops) to boost their production. Special incentives are foreseen
to encourage mobilization of enterprises' own resources, such as income tax
exemption for investments in agriculture; turnover tax exemption and interest
rate subsidies for investment credits to agriculture; reduction of import
duties on agroindustry equipment not produced in Yugoslavia; elimination
of Dinar deposit requirements 1/ with the National Bank for foreign credits
channelled into agricultural investments; and increase in the foreign exchange
retention quota for depreciation charges 2/. On the basis of preliminary dis-
cussions within Government at Federal and Republic levels it is expected that
the total investment for the Plan period would amount to Din 75 billion (US$4.2
billion) in 1975 prices of which the social sector would invest Din 53 billion
and the individual farmer sector Din 22 billion. About 20% of the required
financing is expected to be provided by enterprises or investors' own resources,
40% to be provided by Government and 40% by the Banking system of which about
10-20% are from foreign sources.
2.12      The Agricultural Development Plan, for the first time in Yugoslavia's
postwar development, provides a comprehensive guideline for the development of
agriculture, encompassing both the social and the individual farmer sector. In
recognizing the potential of the individual sector and explicitly supporting
its development, the Plan is a big step towards better resource utilization
and more even income distribution. However, in limiting its support only to
farmers that either form a cooperative or cooperate in some way with the social
sector, the Plan will not reach a substantial portion of the agricultural popu-
lation, notably the smaller farmers and those in the mountainous and infertile
areas.  The Plan's projected growth rate for the social sector (8%) seems
to be optimistic as the Plan fails to recognize that the declining growth
rates in the sector since 1965-66 is not accidental. It indicates a change
in the growth pattern since the social sector with the land and technology
available at present is approaching the level of development where production
increases become more difficult. Because of the two limiting factors growth
of primary production in the social sector is unlikely to exceed 4% per annum
in the next five years. On the other hand the planned annual growth rate of
3% for the individual farmer sector seems achievable. It implies, however, a
growing income discrepancy between the sectors unless the labor force in the
individual farmer sector decreases by about 5%. It is unlikely that migration
of such magnitude would take place. Individual farmers would continue to seek
to increase their incomes through off-farm employment and renting of additional
land to increase their farm size.
1/   Enterprises and banks borrowing abroad are required to deposit a certain
percentage of the foreign credit in dinars with the National Bank with-
out earning interest.
2/   Enterprises are entitled to retain in foreign exchange 10% of the annual
depreciation charges.



- 6 -
III. AGRICULTURAL CREDIT
A. General
3.01      Total Yugoslav agricultural investments in fixed assets and invento-
ries amounted to about Din 6.9 billion annually in the 1971-1975 Plan period,
of which approximately 20% was financed by investors' own funds: 40% by banks
and 40% by budgetary resources.
3.02      Yugoslav enterprises are heavily indebted.  Relative to market eco-
nomy financial practice, liquidity and reinvestment levels are repressed in
Yugoslavia. Factors responsible for this position appear to include: (1) a
relative de-emphais on profit making as an enterprise goal (2) a relatively
greater emphasis on distribution of accounting profits which are shared and
distributed, under the self-management system, by enterprise employees; (3)
a relatively soft approach to financial discipline; and (4) a philosophy of
finance which de-emphasizes problems of risk and does not distinguish strongly
between equity and debt capital in terms of risk taking. The result is that
enterprises have frequently not been in a position to meet the scheduled
demands of creditors, which has resulted in loan rescheduling by banks and
financial reorganization of enterprises, generally by merger, as liquidation
is uncommon.
3.03      Yugoslavia's interest rate policy should be seen in the political
and economic context of the country's socialist self-management system. Under
the present decentralized system, factor prices are beginning to play an in-
creasing role in resource allocation, although the trend toward higher and
more realistic interest rates is delayed by non-economic factors. The coop-
erative basis of the banking structure, where the borrowers are at the same
time the bank's major shareholders (founders) and hence the formulators of
interest rate structures, has contributed to relatively low interest rates.
Furthermore, federal, state and comaunal funds are provided at low cost or
used to subsidize interest rates. Yugoslav authorities consider low interest
rates on investment credits an important policy instrument for development
purposes, especially in the less developed regions and in the agricultural
sector. Also, provisions for bad debts are low as they reflect the partic-
ular relationship between banks and their major borrowers.
3.04      The interest rate structure is approximately as follows:
Deposits (depending upon notice)                            2 to 10%
Short-Term Loans                                           11 to 12%
Commercial Long-Term Loans                                  8 to 12%
Investment Loans from the Federal Fund for Development   3 to 7%
Investment Loans to Agriculture                             3 to 12%



- 7 -
Rates on loans and deposits are fixed each year by the Assembly of Founders
of each bank and vary across the country.
3.05      Present interst rates often yield low or negative real rates which
contribute to a permanent excess demand for investable resources and to the
over capitalization of new investments.  Another result of the excess demand
for capital has been the above-mentioned inability of enterprises to meet
financial obligations on schedule. 1/
3.06      In general, credit is provided to an individual farmer through a
social sector organization with which he has a cooperation agreement (Annex 2,
paras 26-31). This mechanism appears to offer considerable scope for in-
creasing rural incomes, and increasing small farmer access to markets is
a key Project's focus. Individual farmers may also obtain credit directly
from the banks, usually on less favorable terms.
B. The Banking System
3.07      The Basic Economic Report 2/ explains in detail the development of
the Yugoslav Banking System since 1947, and the legal, financial and operational
framework within which banks operate is described in Annex 2. Banks are owned
and controlled by the enterprises (founders) of the region where they operate,
who are also major borrowers from the banks. The organization, ownership,
operations, functions and powers of the banks' management organs and branches
are governed by the statutes and the self-management agreements of each bank.
In addition to its statutes, each bank operates according to an annual
"Business Policy" which implies preparing, against the background of the Five-
Year Plan, a program statement about the Bank's main objectives, magnitude of
operations and interest rates structure. The system appears in general to
work satisfactorily, providing coordination between the investment plans of
enterprises and availability of resources according to priorities as outlined
by the Plan. The banks provide finance for investments according to the Plan
and in particular for agricultural investments according to the priorities
established by the Agricultural Development Plan.
3.08      During the project implementation period the Yugoslav banking
structure will be undergoing a reorganization designed to ensure that the
banks remain responsive to the financial demands of their founders and not
evolve as independent decision making units. The reorganization, based on
a new banking law scheduled for implementation over several years beginning
in 1977, does not represent a departure from the previous approach to the
role of banks, but rather enhances the application of principles which are
fundamental to the Yugoslav view of finance.
1/   See IBRD, The Economic Development of Yugoslavia, Report No. 194a-YU,
November 26, 1973, pp. 285-287.
2/   See IBRD, The Economic Development of Yugoslavia, Report No. 194a-YU,
November 26, 1973, pp. 242-275.



- 8 -
C. Vojvodjanska Banka (Annex 2)
3.09      Vojvodjanska Banka (VB) has served as the apex bank under the First
Project, and it will perform the same role under this project. It is deeply
involved with the agricultural and agro-industrial sector in Vojvodina
Province. VB has its head office in Novi Sad, and its branch network covers
Vojvodina Province, Belgrade and Zagreb. Its head office staff exceeds 225
in number.
3.10      VB's Business Policy and other decisions of its Founders Assembly
guide its operations each year. Investment proposals that enterprises submit
to VB for financing are appraised and approved in a branch office, or in the
head office, depending on the size or complexity of the loans. VB's exposure
or the exposure of any of its branches to a single client should not exceed
30% of total outstanding loans, and no one borrower accounts for more than
10% of its total portfolio. VB operates as a mixed commercial/investment bank
and is authorized to deal in foreign exchange. Supervision of loans, with
regard to borrowers' financial positions and procurement arrangements, is
satisfactory, but could usefully be expanded to include more areas of dialogue
with borrowers. Disbursement procedures are sound.
3.11      VB's staff involved with the First Project have gained experience
in project appraisal methods and have been instrumental in securing more wide-
spread familiarity with these methods throughout Yugoslavia. Continued insti-
tution building activities aimed at appraisal methodology and procurement will
be carried out under this Project.
3.12      The income statement of VB appears in Annex 2, Table 2.  Like other
Yugoslav banks, VB is owned by its founders-borrowers and does not consider
profitability an essential measure of its efficiency and financial performance.
VB has reported positive and growing net earnings in recent years. Income and
expenditure figures are consistent with good administrative control.
3.13      The balance sheet of VB is in Annex 2, Table 1.  VB's assets are
virtually all connected with its banking business, and these have grown
rapidly in recent years. The short term (up'to two years) loan portfolio is
slightly inferior in size to the long term (greater than two years) loan port-
folio, while current liabilities comprise roughly half of total footings.
3.14      VB's forecasts of its financial position indicate increasing profit-
ability and increasing liquidity.
3.15      In accordance with Yugoslav accounting practice, financial statements
of banks are not audited but simply certified by the Federal Social Accounting
Service (SDK) (paras. 6.16-6.17, and Annex 2, paras 11-12). Therefore, an
accurate evaluation of the quality of VB's portfolio and the adequacy of its
provisions is difficult. Its founders have unlimited liability toward VB's
obligations, it is closely integrated with the economy of Vojvodina, and its
financial management has been and is expected to remain satisfactory.



- 9 -
D. The Participating Banks 1/
3.16      One bank from each Republic and Autonomous Province will participate
in the project, and VB would function as participating bank for Vojvodina.
The eight banks, which are among the most important in Yugoslavia, account for
70% to 75% of the total assets of Yugoslavia's business banks.
3.17      The PB's organization and management bodies, including head and
branch offices, are similar to that of VB and have similar operating policies.
However, UBZ, the PB for Croatia, is an association of five independent banks
which are jointly liable for UBZ operations. The PBs are already known to the
Bank from the First Project and from other projects.
3.18      The management and the staff of the banks appear generally satisfac-
tory.  In each PB, the unit responsible for handling long term loans to agri-
culture would be responsible for subloans under the Project; in these units
there are agronomists and agricultural engineers in addition to economists,
financial analysts and usually lawyers. These staff seem to be in general
competent. PBs should be able to adequately appraise and process subloans
under the project without undue delays.
3.19      Appraisal procedures are similar in all banks and have been upgraded
under the First Project. All PBs have adequate staff with the necessary back-
ground and experience to be able to improve further their appraisal techniques
with the help of training efforts under the Project (para 6.03). Supervision
of the subborrowers and procurement and disbursement procedures appear satis-
factory. Development of the scope of supervision to include more of an
advisory or consultative role for the banks in their relationships with their
borrowers would be beneficial, and efforts will be made under the Project to
develop the banks' capacities to serve their borrowers on a broader basis
(Annex 2, para 41).
3.20      All PBs are mixed commercial/investment banks and all are authorized
to deal in foreign exchange. PBs' combined total assets in 1975 exceeded
D 350 billion ($19.5 billion), their deposits exceeded Din 200 billion ($11.1
billion) and their total operating income exceeded Din 22 billion ($1.2 bil-
lion). Of all investment credits to agriculture handled by banks in recent
years, PBs have accounted for a disproportionately large share.
3.21      Short term resources are essentially used for short term invest-
ments, but prudent usage for long term investments is also allowed under the
control of the National Bank, and all the banks in the developed regions and
the Privredna Banka Sarajevo (PBS) do so. Short term loan portfolios and
primary reserves account for approximately 20% to 50% of total banking assets
(Annex 2).
1/   More detailed data on the banks from the four less developed regions
can be found in Report 1058-YU: Appraisal of a Second Industrial Credit
Project, May 11, 1976.



- 10 -
3.22      Financial expenses consume the largest part of the total operating
income of PBs, while administrative expenses have amounted to between 6% and
32% of total operating income in recent years. All of the banks report
profitable operations.
3.23      The balance sheets of PBs show relatively little reliance on net
worth as a source of funds. Primary reserves are generally quite small in
relation to deposit liabilities, and portfolios relative to total liabilities
allow little room for losses. The banks in the less developed regions tend
to be the most liquid and the most oriented towards long term lending, as
a result of their access to Federal and Regional Funds to support priority
investments.
3.24      It is difficult to evaluate accurately the quality of the invest-
ment portfolios of the banks and the adequacy of their provisions, since
data is incomplete and not always entirely consistent. However, the Yugoslav
system has a number of devices, in addition to the role of the central bank,
which protect banks against the threat of liquidation as outlined below.
E.   Creditworthiness of the Banks
3.25      Each of the eight banks is considered a suitable vehicle for imple-
mentation of the Project.  Evaluation of their creditworthiness is difficult,
as some of the banks have not yet been audited against the generally accepted
accounting standards. However, the banks are supported by certain institu-
tional arrangements which have provided sufficient arguments for the approval
of Bank loans supporting agricultural, agro-industrial and industrial projects
in the past. These include:
(a) The founders of each bank have unlimited liability for their
bank's obligations. Creditors of a bank thus have legal
recourse to all assets of founders, many of which are large
industrial enterprises.
(b) Each of the participating banks is closely integrated with
the economy of the area in which it conducts the bulk of its
business, and as such its liquidation is highly improbable.
(c) Each of the banks has performed satisfactorily in the past
within the context of Yugoslav banking.
(d) The bulk of the long-term debt of four of the five smallest
banks consists of funds provided for developmental purposes
by the Federal Republic. It is highly unlikely that the
inability of any bank to repay Federal Funds obligations on
schedule would result in forced liquidation, given the pur-
poses of these funds, the intimate link between these banks
and their founders, and the expected role of their founders
in securing the economic advancement of the areas in which
they operate.



- 11 -
IV. PERFORMANCE UNDER THE FIRST AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
4.01      Performance under the First Project has been generally satisfactory
and the pace of implementation has accelerated over its life. Effectiveness
occurred on February 12, 1976 and by March 31, 1977, 50% of the US$50 million
IBRD loan under the Project had been committed by the participating banks,
with greatest progress in sublending to the individual sector. The entire
loan is expected to be fully committed in the second half of 1977 with only
minor changes in the allocation of investment as specified at appraisal.
Completion will probably be substantially achieved on schedule by June 30,
1979, the present Closing Date.
4.02      Notable progress has been achieved towards attaining a number of
important objectives defined under the first project. These include the
establishment of a nationwide agricultural system with uniform appraisal
criteria, a particularly notable achievement given the complexity and
diversity of the Yugoslav environment. Institution building has also pro-
gressed remarkably well. All Participating Banks (PBs) now have specific
units concerned with evaluating sub-project proposals and have trained
staff in more effective appraisal/supervision techniques. Vojvodjanska
Banka (VB), the lead bank, has exerted considerable influence in the loan
approval process by the prior training and briefing of those in borrowing
kombinats and in PBs who are directly involved in putting together sub-
project proposals. VB's inputs have included training seminars and dis-
cussions, and the exchange of information on an informal basis with PBs
and sub-borrowers.
V. THE PROJECT
A. Objectives
5.01      The Project would provide funds during the three-year investment
period to support the long-term objective of self-sufficiency in and in-
creased exports of those agricultural products for which individual farmer
production is best suited (para 2.04). The Project would help alleviate major
constraints to the further development of livestock and fruit and vegetable
production. It would form part of Yugoslavia's Agricultural Development Plan
and account for about 9.0% of the planned investments during the project's
three-year investment period. It would help achieve the Plan's objectives to
increase production, improve product quality and raise productivity and income,
particularly on private farms.
5.02      The Project places priority on lending to the lesser developed
republics and provinces of Yugoslavia, and compared to the First Project con-
tains an increased emphasis on channeling funds to small individual farmers.
It integrates the lending programs in the individual and the social sectors by
concentrating financing of investments for primary agricultural production in



- 12 -
the individual farmer sector, and reserving the financing of the investments
in social sector to agroindustries directly stimulating private holding pro-
duction. During negotiations assurances were obtained from VB that invest-
ments for agroindustries in the social sector would have at least 50% of their
capacity available for processing or marketing individual farmer produce.
B. Detailed Features
Project Area
5.03      The components financed by the Project would be located throughout
Yugoslavia taking into consideration the less developed Republics and Pro-
vinces as well as the less developed regions of the more developed Republics
and Provinces. While the individual farmer sector has been relatively neg-
lected in the past, it would receive, under the Project, 57% of the loan
proceeds as compared to 43% under the First Agricultural Credit Project.
Regional and sectoral allocations were agreed upon by the Republics and Pro-
vinces taking into account the agricultural development priorities, regional
income and sectoral distribution objectives and will be enforced by VB and the
PBs. During negotiations assurances were obtained that it will be enforce
the regional and sectoral distribution of the Bank loan as shown in Annex 6.
Vegetable Production
5.04      Subloans would be provided to individual farmers to develop about
2,200 ha for labor intensive mixed vegetable production and about 40 ha of
plastic greenhouses for early vegetable production (Annex 3). The Project for
mixed vegetable production would finance pumps, pipes, wells and other equip-
ment to construct small scale on-farm irrigation systems. It would also
include the purchase of motor cultivators and implements such as trailers,
ploughs tillers and sprayers. For construction of plastic houses the Project
would provide for tubing, plastic covering and other items. Increased vege-
table production would mainly supply the domestic market. Some early and
processed vegetables would be exported.
Fruit Production
5.05      Subloans would be provided in the Project to private farmers for
development of about 6,500 ha of orchards including 10% of soft fruit plant-
ings and of about 1,500 ha of vineyards of which 500 ha would be mainly table
grapes (Annex 3). Included would be production of fruits for which there
exists strong domestic or export demand, such as raspberries, cherries,
peaches, apples and grapes. Investment would include planting materials,
fertilizers, land preparation and small motor cultivators for transport,
tillage, and spraying. To avoid overplanting of vineyards which might result
in overproduction of wine, the Project would restrict its financing of wine
vineyards to replacement of old vineyards.



- 13 -
Land Development
5.06      Subloans would be provided in the Project to individual farmers to
finance about 5,500 ha of Land Development (Annex 3). Investment would be
for clearing land, land levelling, drainage, access roads and consolidation
of land. Included would be surveying to establish boundaries, machine ser-
vices, fertilizers and consolidation of small parcels into larger plots to
increase productivity through better land utilization. The works would most
probably be undertaken within the framework of a larger land development
scheme of the social sector. The Project would finance part of the works
or the facilities which are deemed to service small farms.
Livestock Production
5.07      The Project would finance livestock farms in all the republics and
provinces. The typical investments are described in Annex 4. They include
construction of cattle stables, pig barns, sheep barns, poultry houses, silo
and slurry pits, the purchase of heifers, ewes and rams and equipment, includ-
ing tractor and implements, silo combines, milking machines and other farm
equipment.
5.08      Dairy Farms.  This is one of the major components of the Project
(23.0%) which would help achieve one of the Plan priority objectives of self-
sufficiency in milk production. The Project would provide for the purchase
of about 11,200 in-calf heifers and dairy cows, primarily Simmenthal and
Freisian breeds, of which about 5,600 head would be imported. Calves would
be sold at about 10 months of age after they have reached a weight of about
320 kg. Culled cows would be replaced annually by farmers own production
of heifers. Semen from Government artificial insemination centers would be
used for breeding. About 2,950 farmers would be financed under the Project.
5.09      Cattle Fattening Farms.  The Project would provide for construc-
tion of fattening barns equipped with drinkers and feeders, silos, and farm
machinery for feed production. About 500 farmers would be financed under the
Project. Feeder calves would be purchased at about 220 kg and after feeding
for about 180 days, they would be sold at 450 kg. Feed would consist of
sillage produced by the farmers, and formulated concentrate mixture provided
by the kombinats or the cooperatives. About 30% of the production would be
for export. Because of the volatility of the beef export market each sub-
project consisting of bulked investment plans of several individual farmers,
wou'ld have to provide a detailed market analysis and would have to be approved
by the Bank prior to its financing. It is estimated that at least fifty in-
dividual farmers would be included in each subproject proposed by the kombinat
receiving project funds.
5.10      Pig Fattening Farms.  The Project would develop pig production in
Serbia and Vojvodina. The investments financed include pig barns, feeders
and drinkers and other farm equipment. Kombinats or cooperatives which would
be purchasing fattened animals from the farmers, would supply them with pig-
lets and mixed feed on credit and would provide them with veterinary services
and other technical advice. About 800 farmers would be financed under the
Project. The total pork production is expected to be consumed locally.



- 14 -
5.11      Sheep Breeding and Fattening Farms.  The investments under this
component would include construction of winter housing and feeding facilities,
farm machinery including tractors in the case of sheep fattening farms, pas-
ture improvement to extend the grass season and increase hay production for
winter feeding and breeding stocks of which about 50% would be imported.
The component aims at increasing the productivity and income of a segment
of farmers living in remote areas of the country, with traditionally little
access to credit and marketing facilities. About 1,500 such farmers would be
financed under the Project.
5.12      Broiler Farms.  This component is a part of a broiler production
project in Kosovo, integrating the production of individual farmers, and pro-
duction and processing of poultry by a kombinat (para 5.15). The investment
would include buildings of the deep litter type, with adequate ventilation and
heating facilities. The kombinat would supply farmers with breeding stocks,
suitable mixed feed on credit, veterinary services and other technical advice,
and the farmers would in return sell their production to the slaughterhouse
operated by the kombinat. Farmers are expected to supply at least 50% of the
raw material needed for the slaughterhouse and the broiler processing facili-
ties of the kombinat (para 5.15). About 1,000 farmers would be financed under
the Project.
Agricultural Industries
5.13      In keeping with government policies, project financing for agro-
industries would focus mainly on milk, meat and fruits and vegetables pro-
cessing, all in the social sector. Following is a summary of the projected
subloans in each category; details of which are in Annex 5.
5.14      Milk Processing.  The Project would finance about 1,450 milk col-
lecting stations and about 35 insulated milk-tank-trucks to organize milk
collection which is a critical feature in Croatia, Bosnia-Herzegovina, Kosovo
and Macedonia. Financing would also be available for construction of four
dairy plants and expansion or modernization of about 14 existing plants. All
Republics, excluding Vojvodina Province, would participate. The equivalent
capacity involved would be about 40 million liters per annum (about 4% of
Yugoslavia's total milk production).
5.15      Meat Processing.  The meat processing sectors suffer from outdated
and unhygienic plants. Finance would be provided for expansion or moderniza-
tion of four plants in Bosnia and Herzegovina and three plants in Montenegro.
The equivalent capacity would represent about 3,200 tons of carcass beef pro-
cessed per year. In Kosovo an integrated broiler production and processing
facility would be financed. The complex would produce 2.5 million day old
chicks annually of which 1.5 million would be reared into broilers by the
individual farmer sector (1,000 farmers) and 1 million by the complex itself.
The slaughtering would have a capacity of 2.5 million birds for processing
the production from both sectors.
5.16      Fruit and Vegetable Processing.  This sector would receive the
largest financial support due to the growing popularity of these commodities
in Yugoslavia and the export potential offered by some of the West-European



- 15 -
countries, notably the German Federal Republic. The Project would finance
three cold stores with a combined capacity exceeding 9,000 tons in Serbia.
The cold stores would have adjacent fruit grading and packing facilities. In
addition a total of ten plants would be expanded and/or modernized for produc-
tion of frozen, and pickled vegetables and fruit juices in all of the other
republics and provinces except Croatia. The total capacity of these plants
would exceed 50,000 tons annually. To process the increasing winegrape pro-
duction of Macedonia, a winery of 10 million liters would also be financed
in that Republic.
5.17      Grain Dryers.  About 7 grain dryers of a 30,000 ton/each yearly
throughput would be financed in Vojvodina, the largest cereal producing area
of the Federation.
C. Cost Estimates
5.18      Total Project cost is estimated at Din 4,156 million (US$231 million)
of which 32% or Din 1,350 million (US$75 million) would be foreign exchange.
Details of investment costs are shown in Annexes 3, 4 and 5, and are summarized
below.
5.19      Cost estimates used in the report are based on January 1977 costs.
An average 5.0% physical contingency and an average 20.0% price contingency
have been included in the Project costs to meet expected inflation in foreign
and local costs during the implementation period. Duties and taxes are in-
cluded in total project cost and are estimated to amount to about Din 308
million.

D. Financing
5.20      The financing plan of the Project is shown below:

5.21      The IBRD loan of US$75 million representing the total estimated for-
eign exchange cost of the project, is about 32% of total Project cost. The
loan would be made to VB for 15 years, including three years of grace, for
on-lending to subborrowers through a PB in each Republic and Province (paras.
6.05, 6.06). The Project cash flow (Annex 7) shows that subborrowers' repay-
ments would generate sufficient inflow to repay the IBRD loan within 15 years
and to cover a reasonable degree of slippage in repayment. About 22% of the
total Project cost would be contributed by subborrowers, 46% by the PBs and
32% by IBRD. For the investment in primary production to be undertaken in
the individual sector, 20% of the total Project cost would be contributed by
subborrowers, 52% from the PBs and 28% from the proposed IBRD loan. For the
agroindustrial investments to be undertaken by the social sector enterprises
25% of the investment cost would be contributed by the subborrowers, 34% by
the PBs and the remaining 41% would be provided by the proposed IBRD loan.
Assurances were obtained during negotiations on the aforesaid conditions.
E. Procurement
5.22      Procurement under the project would be done by subborrowers under
the supervision of VB and PBs. VB would have supervisory responsibilities
for all ICB procurement arrangements organized by the PBs. Assurances were



- 18 -
obtained at negotiations that it would appoint on terms and conditions satis-
factory to the Bank, a procurement specialist to the staff of its Project Unit
to assist with all aspects of procurement under the project. The bulking of
small items for procurement under the prescribed procedures will be organized
by the PBs in their respective republics and by VB on an inter-republic basis
when such a possibility appears feasible. The following items relating to
project investments in the social sector would be suitable for international
competitive bidding according to IBRD guidelines: grain driers, fruit and
vegetable grading, packing equipment and cold storages, milk processing
equipment, collecting stations and tank trucks, the integrated broiler facility
and the winery. The integrated broiler facility and the cold stores contracts
would be let on a turnkey basis. Contract tendering for the equipment and
materials agreed upon would be advertised internationally, and Yugoslavian
manufacturers would be allowed a 15% perference margin or the applicable
customs duty, whichever is lower. It is estimated that items to be procured
under international competitive bidding would total approximately US$50 mil-
lion of which about US$10 million would probably be awarded directly to non-
Yugoslav suppliers.
5.23      Procurement of items for fruit and vegetable production, livestock
and livestock facility and land development on individuals' farms, as well as
minor civil works and equipment for processing facilities involving contracts
of an amount not exceeding the equivalent of US$100,000 would be made through
existing commercial channels under negotiated purchase or other procedures as
specified locally. Procurement of all other items, except as noted in para
5.22, would be handled through local competitive bidding procedures already
determined to be satisfactory to the Bank. These items would not be suitable
for bulk purchase and international competitive bidding because of phasing of
subprojects; the dispersion of investors throughout the country; and in cer-
tain cases of expansion and modernization of processing facilities, the advan-
tages of ensuring compatibility with equipment presently in place. Existing
commercial channels, which include domestic and international suppliers, main-
tain generally adequate supplies of spare parts, providing servicing and re-
pair facilities and are suitably competitive. For the contracts procured
under local competitive bidding and exceeding US$250,000 equivalent VB would
furnish the Bank promptly after execution, two confirmed copies of such con-
tracts together with analysis of the respective bids received and recommenda-
tions for award. For the contracts procured under local competitive bidding
exceeding US$100,000 but less than US$250,000 equivalent VB and PBs will: (i)
retain such contracts and will make them available for review by IBRD during
supervision; and (ii) furnish to IBRD a report containing an analysis of bids
and the recommendation and justification for the award including a certifica-
tion that local procurement procedures have been followed. For the contract
procured under local competitive bidding but not exceeding US$100,000 equiva-
lent VB and PBs will retain such contracts for IBRD review during supervision
but will furnish to IBRD along with applications for withdrawals a certifica-
tion that local procurement procedures have been followed. During negotiations
assurances were obtained from VB that it will enforce and cause PBs to enforce
the preceding procurement procedures.



- 19 -
F. Disbursements
5.24      IBRD disbursements would take about 3 years although the entire loan
is expected to be committed by the end of year 2. The annual phasing of invest-
ments and the forecast of quarterly disburements are shown in Annexes 7 and 8.
5.25      Disbursement of the IBRD loan would be made against 35% of subloan
amounts disbursed by VB to the PBs on the account of subloans made for invest-
ments in individual farms, and 55% on the account of subloans made for invest-
ments in social sector (para 5.20). The Bank would disburse against certifi-
cates of expenditure for all subloans. The same procedures would apply for
disbursement by VB to the PBs.
G. Environmental Impact
5.26      The Project is not expected to have significant adverse environ-
mental impact on the Project area.
VI. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. Organization
6.01      The institutional structure of the proposed project is identical
to that developed and utilized under the First Agricultural Credit Project,
and the risks of non-performance and implementational difficulties are there-
fore very substantially reduced.
The Federal Coordination Committee (FCC)
6.02      The Federal Coordination Committee coordinates agricultural invest-
ments in Yugoslavia, but has no "line" responsibilities. It was established
under the Agricultural Development Plan. Its members are representatives of
the Secretariats for Agriculture of the Republics and Autonomous Provinces
and of the Federal Government with the Deputy Chairman of the Federal Com-
mittee for Agriculture (i.e., the Ministry of Agriculture) as chairman.
Assurances were obtained during negotiations that the Committee would con-
tinue to perform its coordination role with respect to the Project including
the responsibility for policy questions and reallocation of proceeds of the
loan between Republics and Provinces and sectors, as under the First Project.
6.03      VB's organization, financial condition and operating policies are
adequate for efficient implementation of the Project (paras 3.09-3.15). The
Project Operations Department established in VB under the First Project has
had primary implementational responsibility and has performed satisfactorily.
Assurances were obtained during negotiations that the Department would con-
tinue to operate in VB, that a Procurement Specialist would be added to its
staff, that it would remain adequately staffed and responsible for appraisal



- 20 -
and supervision of all subloans under the First and the Second Projects. With
respect to its own staff, PB staff and the staff of social sector subborrowers,
VB would continue its training role (para 3.19).
6.04      As the borrower and apex bank, VB would be responsible for the repay-
ment of the proposed loan and the proper execution of the Project. It is esti-
mated that the cost of operations of the Project Operations Department would be
about Din 10 million over the three year Project implementation period. VB's
risk on its loans to the PBs is negligible and VB is not expected to make a
profit on its loans to the PBs. To help meet the cost of its operations in
administering the Project, VB would receive a commission of 1% on IBRD funds
disbursed at the time of disbursement. VB's lending rate on its loans to PBs
out of the proceeds of the Bank loan will be identical to the rate at which it
receives funds from IBRD. The grace and repayment period would be such that
each repayment date of the subloan to VB would be three months before the
corresponding repayment date by VB to the Bank. The VB's commitment fee to
the PB's will be 0.75%. These provisions would be covered under the subsi-
diary loan agreement. Assurances to this effect were obtained from VB during
negotiations.
The Participating Banks
6.05      The bank from each Republic and Province included in the First
Project (listed in Annex 2, para 35) would participate under the Project and
VB would function as PB for the Autonomous Province of Vojvodina. The banks'
performance under the First Project has been generally satisfactory. A summary
appraisal of the PBs is given in paras 3.21 to 3.24. The PBs, including VB,
would be directly responsible for the execution of the Project in their re-
spective Republic of Province. A PB will normally finance, under the Project,
only subprojects in its respective Republic or Province. Each PB would receive
from VB a subloan out of the proceeds of the Bank loan according to its allo-
cation (para 5.03). The PBs would present appropriate documentation for reim-
bursement of the Bank portion of the subloans to VB for review and submission
to the Bank for disbursements.
6.06      A subsidiary loan agreement will be concluded between each PB and VB
covering the policies and procedures of sublending under the Project, project
related staffing of the PBs, and the arrangements for procurement, disburse-
ment, audit and monitoring. Signing of the subsidiary loan agreement by the
seven PBs under terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank, is a condition
of effectiveness of the Bank loan. VB, in its function as PB for Vojvodina,
would follow the same sublending, procurement, disbursement, audit and monitor-
ing procedures. Assurances to this effect were obtained during negotiations.
B. Policies and Procedures of Projects Sublending
Appraisal Criteria of Subprojects
6.07      Subprojects in the individual farmer sector would normally be sub-
mitted to the PBs by social sector enterprises, kombinats or cooperatives which
aggregate investment proposals of several farmers with whom they have entered



- 21 -
into a cooperation agreement and borrow on their behalf. However, individual
farmers may also submit subproject investment proposals directly to a PB.
In appraising subprojects in the individual farmer sector, VB and PBs would
satisfy themselves that:
(a) subprojects are technically feasible, financially viable,
commercially sound and economically justified, and markets
and processing facilities for their incremental production
are available;
(b) subprojects and subborrowers would have adequate debt
capacities in that they would generate sufficient cash flow
to cover debt service after meeting legal claims and appro-
priate reinvestment levels;
(c) adequate and continuing technical assistance, and suitable
prices and market outlets are available to farmers who are
subborrowers.
(d) in the case of lending to individual farmers through social
sector organizations, the cooperation agreements are satis-
factory in all respects.
6.08      In appraising subprojects in the social sector, VB and the PBs
would ensure that:
(a) Subprojects are technically feasible, financially viable,
commercially sound and economically justified, and that
markets and processing facilities for their incremental
production are available;
(b) At least 50% of the processing capacities of subprojects
would be used for individual farmer production;
(c)  subprojects and subborrowers have adequate debt capacity in
that they would generate sufficient cash flow to cover debt
service after meeting legal claims, compulsory allocations to
the enterprise's funds and appropriate reinvestment levels.
Subprojects should achieve attractive financial rates of
return. A particular justification will be required if the
financial rate of return is below 12% in constant Dinars;
(d) Enterprises borrowing for subprojects have a satisfactory
financial structure, especially an adequate debt/equity
ratio of normally less than 3:1. Exceptions may be al-
lowed (based on the financial and economic merit of the
subproject) provided that the enterprise enters into an
agreement with a PB to increase its equity over a suitable
period of time to an acceptable level;



- 22 -
(e)  Appropriate consideration is given to the choice of tech-
nology, with preference accorded to more labor intensive
methods as opposed to capital-intensive, automated tech-
nology; and to the consequences of such a choice on the
investment costs of the subprojects.
Assurances were obtained from VB during negotiations that procedures as
outlined in paras. 6.07 and 6.08 would be followed by VB and the PBs in
appraising subprojects.
The Subloans
6.09      The social sector sub-borrowers would provide a minimum of 25% of
investment costs and individual farmer subborrowers would provide a minimum
of 20% of investment costs, defined to include only incremental investments
in project-supported activities. The PBs would provide the remaining amounts
with the Bank reimbursing them for 55% and 35% of their loans for social
sector subborrowers and individual sector sub-borrowers respectively. Prior
to disbursing the Bank loan, VB would assure the availability of counterpart
funds and subborrowers' contribution for each specific subproject.
6.10      Seasonal credit would be provided by the PBs or the respective com-
mercial banks of subborrowers where appropriate. Assurances were obtained
during negotiations that VB would, prior to requesting disbursement of the
Bank Loan, be satisfied that the necessary amount of seasonal credit would
be available for each subproject and that financing plans set forth above in
para 6.09 are adhered to by the PBs and VB.
Lending Terms
6.11      The terms of subloans under the project would be related to the
cash flow projections of the investments (Annexes 3, 4 and 5). Assurances
were obtained during negotiations that the following grace periods will not
be exceeded; and that repayment periods on the subloans will not exceed the
following, except as justified by projections of subproject cash flow at the
time the loan commitment is undertaken by a PB.

Interest Rates
6.12      As detailed in Chapter 3, interest rates for investments in agri-
culture vary between 3% and 12%, depending upon the Republic or Province, the
source of funds and the type of investment. These levels of interest rates
and the variations in interest rates between regions, banks, similar invest-
ments and sectors create inefficiencies and inconsistencies in resource allo-
cation. Assurances were obtained at negotiations that the Association of



- 23 -
Yugoslav Banks will take all appropriate action before December 1, 1977 to
undertake a study on interest rate, under terms and conditions satisfactory
to the Bank. The study will particularly review in depth selected financial
intermediation topics including the role of interest rates. Proposed terms
of reference for such study are in Annex 2 Appendix 2. Assurances were also
obtained at negotiations that an interest rate reform will be undertaken by
PBs, so that rates on domestic resources loaned under the Project to social
sector subborrowers will be at least 6% and to individual sector subborroers
at least 5%. The lending rate to be applied to the IBRD portion of subproject
finance is 11%. These rates are expected to result in minimum blend rates of
approximately 8.75% and 7.10% for social and individual sector subsectors,
respectively. The rate structure would provide the PBs with a spread of about
2.5-3.0% on the proceeds of the Bank loan and usually at least 1.20% on the
local funds portion of the subloans to cover the banks' administrative costs.
6.13      The foreign exchange risk arrangements agreed upon for the First
Project will continue to apply under the proposed Project. Social sector
subborrowers would carry the full foreign exchange risk with respect to the
portion of subloans financed out of the proceeds of the Bank loan for their
own investments. The foreign exchange risk on subloans to individual farmers
would be carried by the Federal Republic. It is also expected that VB and the
PBs would protect themselves against the exchange risk on the foreign exchange
accruing to them from subloan repayments occuring earlier than amortization
payments of the Bank loan. Assurances to this effect were obtained from the
Federal Government and VB during negotiations.
Appraisal Procedures of Subprojects
6.14      Investment proposals would be submitted by enterprises to the PBs
in their Republic or Province. Several investment proposals in the individual
farmer sector would usually be aggregated and submitted by a social sector
organization. Individual farmers may also submit a proposal directly to a PB.
The PBs would apppraise the subprojects according to the criteria set forth
in paras 6.09 and 6.10 and the PBs would register each subproject with VB.
As under the First Project, the following procedures would be applied:
(a) the PBs would approve all subprojects having an investment
cost below US$1 million;
(b) VB would review the appraisal of and approve all subprojects
having an investment cost between US$1 million and US$1.5
million;
(c) the Bank would review the appraisal of and approve all sub-
projects having an investment cost above US$1.5 million.
Apart from the subprojects costing more than US$1.5 million, the Bank would
also review and approve the appraisal of all cattle fattening subprojects
(para 5.09). Assurances were obtained during negotiations that these proce-
dures would be followed.



- 24 -
C. Project Monitoring
Audit
6.16      Assurances were obtained during negotiations that VB and all PBs
would each follow the auditing procedures set out below in paras 6.16 through
6.18. They would maintain a separate account for the project. This would
include disbursements made under the project for all subloans.
6.17      As required by Yugoslav law all banks and enterprises are audited
annually by the Social Accounting Service (SDK). Normal audit by SDK, which
is limited to verification of cash balances and checking of financial state-
ments for mathematical accuracy and comparisons with previous years does not
conform to internationally accepted standards.  SDK has implemented a training
program for its staff, with the Bank's support, to undertake internationally
accepted audits of Yugoslav banks.  Full audits for all PBs will not become
available until after the end of the disbursement period of the Project, given
the number of banks involved in SDK's program and the difficulties in verifi-
cation posed by the scope of the banks' operations and the absence of conven-
tional auditing by international standards at the founders' level.
6.18      The subsidiary loan agreement would provide that during the tran-
sition period, i.e., before satisfactory audits can be performed, the PBs
would submit through VB to the Bank, SDK certified financial statements
acceptable to the Bank, within six months of the end of the fiscal year. The
subsidiary agreement would also provide that, when satisfactory audits can
be performed, financial statements audited in accordance with international
standards would be submitted by the PBs through VB to the Bank within six
months of the end of each fiscal year.
Supervision
6.19      Assurances were obtained during negotiations that VB and the PBs
would ensure that adequate technical and financial supervision of on-going
subprojects would be carried out. The social sector subborrowers and the
social sector enterprises borrowing on behalf of individual farmers would
submit to the respective PB, SDK-certified financial statements within six
months from the end of each fiscal year. Basic supervision would be done
by the PBs under the general responsibility of VB.
Reporting and Monitoring Requirements
6.20      Assurances were obtained during negotiations that VB will provide the
Bank with quarterly and annual reports covering the progress of the project,
sublending operations under the project, financial data pertaining to the
project, the financial statements of VB and the PBs and general data on
agriculture and ecnomic policy affecting the implementation of the project.
VB wiould also be responsible for evaluating the project's benefits at the
level of social sector subborrowers and also at the level of individual
farmer subborrowers. Evaluation is to involve field surveys as well as
examination of data pertaining to the production and financial performance
of social sector investments under the Project. Assurances were obtained



- 25 -
during negotiations that as a condition of loan effectiveness, VB and PB's
will have secured all assistance satisfactory to the Bank for the purpose
of preparing suitable monitoring procedures to apply to the First as well as
the Second Agricultural Credit Projects and that VB and PBs will take all
appropriate action to implement promptly on or before March 31, 1978 such
monitoring procedures as agreed upon with the Bank.
VII. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
A. Production, Markets and Prices
Production
7.01      The following table shows the incremental annual production gen-
erated by the Project (Annexes 3, 4 and 5):
