The total value of annual incremental production at full development is
estimated at Din 2.4 million (US$135.2 million) at January 1977 prices.
Markets
7.02      The incremental production derived from the Project would serve
the following purposes: additional fodder production would result in a much
needed increase in livestock feed supplies and help reduce imports of feed
grains. Incremental vegetable production would be needed predominantly
to supply increasing domestic demand while the additional fruit production
would equally help promote exports as well as satisfy rising domestic needs.
The increase in milk production would allow the extremely low level of per
capita consumption to rise. Increased beef production would supply both the
domestic and foreign, particularly Western European markets. With the strong
demand for Project commodities largely in the domestic markets, the incre-
mental production at full development would not pose any particular marketing
problem, except possibly for the cattle fattening component, for which market
prospects might fluctuate from year to year. The appraisals of cattle fatten-
ing subprojects will include a detailed market analysis, and should be sub-
mitted to the Bank for approval, irrespective of the free limit arrangements
outlined in para 6.14.



- 26 -
Prices
7.03      The incremental production from the Project, which would result in
substituting for or reducing the growth of imports, or supplying otherwise not
satisfied needs, would not induce a reduction in producer prices since the
expected demand increase is likely to exceed the additional production. Thus
rising farmgate prices due to excess demand are expected, but they would
increase less than without the Project.
B. Financial Benefits
7.04      The financial results for typical investment models under the Proj-
ect are given in Annexes 3, 4 and 5. The calculations are based on January
1977 Yugoslav prices for both inputs and outputs, which are generally in line
with international prices. The financial rates of return of the models vary
between 16% and 37%. The sensitivity analysis yields the following: if
investment costs were raised by 10% the financial rates of return would vary
between 15% and 33%: an increase in operating expenses by 10% would result
in financial rates of return between 0% and 24%; and if revenues were to drop
by 10% the rates would range from 0% to 20%.
7.05      Some investments are extremely sensitive to an increase in operating
costs or decrease in revenues, notably those in cattle fattening. As mentioned
above (para 7.02), these subprojects would be reviewed by the Bank to ensure
their financial viability. The financial rates of return of the other invest-
ment models have remained satisfactory under sentivity testing, as it can be
expected that inflationary prices rises would affect both costs and benefits
and tend to maintain the present relationship between them. In any case sub-
projects yielding financial rates of return of less than 12% would not be
approved by VB and the PBs (para 6.08).
7.06      The average annual income of individual farmers would increase from
Din 39,000 to Din 86,000 (or from US$2,170 to US$4,780). The Project would,
through the investments in milk collection stations, cold storage, grain
dryers facilities and slaughterhouses, also benefit another 40,000 small
farms by raising their incomes by an average of about Din 20,000 a year.
C. Economic Benefits
Foreign Exchange Earnings
7.07      The Project in concentrating on the domestic market would, over its
20 year lifetime, generate about US$210 million in foreign exchange through
increased exports. Taking into account foreign exchange savings through import
substitution (mainly feed grains and milk products) and direct and indirect
import components of investments and operating costs, the total net foreign
exchange earnings or savings, over the Project's 20 year lifetime, are esti-
mated to amount to the order of US$400 million (at 1977 prices).



- 27 -
Employment
7.08      The Project would generate substantial employment opportunities.
An estimated 4,200 incremental manyears would be generated from investments in
the social and about 6,000 manyears in the individual farmer sector annually.
For an additional 16,800 farmers, the Project would reduce underemployment and
provide means to use more fully their free capacities in farming.
7.09      About 8,600 additional manyears would be generated during the 3
years investment period: 4,500 manyears in the social sector for building
and construction work and engineering services, and 4,100 manyears in the
private sector for expanding buildings, establishing fruit trees and vine-
yards and digging wells. The additional investments and consumption of
inputs, such as fertilizer, pesticides, fuel, via their backward linkages,
and the increased production via forward linkages, would generate sizeable
indirect employment opportunities, which were not quantified.
Project Beneficiaries
7.10      A significant share of the Project's financing would benefit the
poorer farmers. About US$43 million or 57% of the proposed Bank loan would
be directed towards assisting an estimated 16,800 small farmers (of which
about 50% would be located in less developed regions) to undertake selected
on-farm investments. The remaining US$32 million or 43% of the proposed Bank
loan would affect the income of at least an additional 40,000 individual
farmers by enhancing their access to markets and related services through
cooperation with the social sector. Approximately 60% of project beneficia-
ries in the individual farmer sector are in the target group, with preproject
incomes not exceeding Din 6,138 (US$341) per family member. In addition,
approximately 4,000 workers in project-assisted social sector enterprises
would benefit from the Project.
Resource Allocation
7.11      The Project in involving eight major banks would bring about sub-
stantial benefits from improved resource allocation, particularly in the less
developed regions. The staff of VB and the PBs, which would be trained under
this Project, is likely to handle most of the credit financed investments in
the agricultural sector. Their experience gained under the Project could
result in significant savings of investment resources in the agricultural
sector.
Rate of Return
7.12      The economy is expected to realize a return of 22% from total Project
investments, including physical contingencies (Annex 9). Although the adminis-
trative and training costs are included in the Project costs, their benefits
cannot be quantified. Hired labor has been valued at prevailing wage rates.
Family labor has not been costed as operating expenses because there are unused
labor capacities on private farms. However, family labor devoted to invest-
ment activities has been costed at prevailing wage rates. Wheat, maize, wool,



- 28 -
tobacco and beef were projected at world market price levels. Other commodi-
ties were valued at Janaury 1977 farmgate prices, adjusted for import duties,
taxes and subsidies. A sensitivity analysis was applied to test the Project
against changes in investment costs, operating expenses and production results.
By increasing investment costs and operating expenses alternatively by 10%,
the rate of return would drop to 20% and 15% respectively. By decreasing the
value of the benefits by 10% the overall return would drop to 12%. The eco-
nomic rates of return for individual components for fruit and vegetable, live-
stock development and agroindustries component are 25%, 23%, and 18% respec-
tively. Sensitivity analysis applied to the individual benefit and cost
streams of these respective components show that they remain viable (Annex 9).
VIII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
8.01      During negotiations assurances were obtained from Federal Govern-
ment, as the guarantor that it will cause the Association of Yugoslav Banks
to take all appropriate action before December 1, 1977 to undertake a study
on interest rate on terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank (para 6.12).
8.02      During negotiations the principal assurances obtained from VB, as
the Borrower were that:
(i) it will follow and cause the PBs to follow the financing
conditions stated in paras 6.09 through 6.14; and
(ii) it will follow and cause the PBs to follow the procurement
and monitoring procedures contained in paras 5.22 and 5.23
and paras 6.16 through 6.20.
8.03      Conditions for making the proposed loan effective are:
(i) the signing of the subsidiary loan agreement between VB and
PBs, satisfactory to the Bank (para 6.06);
(ii) the implementation of interest rate policy described in para
6.12; and
(iii) the securement of appropriate assistance to prepare a set of
monitoring procedures (para 6.20).
8.04      With the indicated agreements and fulfillment of the above condi-
tions, the Project would be suitable for a Bank loan of US$75 million,
equivalent, for a term of 15 years including a three year grace period.



ANNEX I

APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
The Agricultural Sector
A. Description of the Natural and Institutional Framework
1.        Geography and Climate.  The Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia
(SFRY) is located beween latitudes 400 and 470 north and longitudes 130 and 230
east. As a federal state it consists of six Socialist Republics: Bosnia-
Herzegovina, Croatia, Macedonia, Montenegro, Serbia Proper and Slovenia; and
two Autonomous Provinces attached to Serbia: Vojvodina and Kosovo. It borders
Italy, Austria, Hungary, Romania, Bulgaria, Greece, Albania and the Adriatic
Sea.
2.        Of Yugoslavia's total territory of 255,804 km2, about 58% is recorded
as agricultural land while the rest is taken up by forests, rivers and lakes.
Of the agricultural land about 70% is cultivable and the rest consists of perma-
nent pastures. The extremely fertile soils predominantly located in Vojvodina
and the Northern regions of Croatia represent only one-sixth of the country's
total area but contain nearly a third of the arable land and produce more than
half of the country's output of wheat and maize and over three-quarters of its
sugarbeet. This region is also suitable for intensive livestock and vegetable
production. In the hills (up to 500 meters in altitude) which account for about
40% of the total area and cover much of Serbia, Slovenia and Bosnia, a mixed
farm pattern prevails. Important lines of production are livestock raising
and fruit growing and, to a lesser degree, field crops. The mountain region
covering 44% of the country is generally unfavorable for crop production, but
extensive upland pastures support sheep and some cattle raising. At the
Adriatic coast, vineyards and olive and citrus groves are found, whereas in
southern Macedonia sub-tropical crops, such as tobacco, cotton and rice are
grown.
3.        In the valleys and plains alluvial soils are predominant with cher-
nozem prevailing in most of Vojvodina and the northeastern part of Croatia.
Delluvial and terra rossa soils are common to the hills. Soil erosion is a
major problem on steep, barren hills and mountain slopes. Cultivated soils
are usually well supplied with organic matter and potash, but poor in phos-
phates. The pH ranges in most areas from acid to neutral; slightly alkaline
soils are located in the northern part of the country.
4.        There are three basic climatic zones in Yugoslavia:  (i) the Mediter-
ranean, (ii) the modified Atlantic, and (iii) the continental climatic zones.
Due to the topographic configuration of the country these zonal climates are
much modified by the terrain. The mediterranean climate of the Adriatic



ANNEX 1
Page 2
coast has mild winters and hot summers (annual average temperatures 150C),
with relatively heavy rainfall (e.g. Dubrovnik 1,500 mm), characteristically
in the autumn and winter months while the summer are dry. The modified
atlantic climate of Slovenia and northwestern Croatia is the coldest in the
country (annual average 90C). These areas receive heavy rainfall (e.g.
Ljubljana 1,54 mm), which is lowest in winter and relatively evenly spread
during the other seasons. The continental climate is most characteristic of
Vojvodina. Here, too, the winters are vey cold, but the summers are quite hot.
Precipitation in this area is among the lowest in the country (e.g. Vrsac
682 mm), but tends to be concentrated in late spring and early summer, which
allows high plant utilization in the period of greatest growth. A temperate
continental climate exists between the continental and mediterranean zones,
covering essentially the whole of the Dinaric System, which has the lowest
annual average temperatures, with cold winters and cool summerE, and heavy
precipitation (e.g. Kolasin 2,670 mm) concentrated in the autumn and winter.
Another transitional zone also exists, which includes most of Macedonia, where
the Continental influence is basic and the Mediterranean secondary.
5.        Land Use.  Total agricultural land decreased slightly from 14.8 mil-
lion ha to 14.5 million ha during the past ten years. Though the area under
cereals decreased by about 10% (500,000 ha) total production of cereals in-
creased because of higher yields. The decrease in cereal cultivation is partly
due to the general decrese of agricultural land and partly because of an expan-
sion of lands under fallow and an expansion of lands under more profitable
crops such as vegetables, industrial crops and fruit. Land use in Yugoslavia
in 1975 was as follows (Table 1):
Land Use in SFRY in 1975
Percent of        Percent of       Percent of
Agric. Land      Cultiv. Land      Arable Land
Agricultural Land                    100
Cultivate Land                        69.1            100

Fish Pond, Pools,
Roads, etc.                          0.6

Orchards                                                 4.7
Vineyards                                                2.7
Meadows                                                 19.6

Vegetables (incl. potatoes)                                                8.9

ANNEX 1
Page 3
6.        Though the total area under cereals declined its structure did not
undergo important changes. Maize and wheat cover more than 80 percent of the
land under grain. Sunflower and sugarbeets are the two most important indus-
trial crops. The area under sunflower has increased by more than 25% since
1966 reaching about 45% in 1975.  Land under vegetable production is increas-
ing, with potatoes covering about 50% of the land under vegetables.
7.        As described in paras 2 and 3 above, the land use characteristics is
largely dictated by soil quality, topography and water availability. Irriga-
tion is however another important influence on cropping patterns, although
only 1.5% (150,000 ha) of total cultivated land in Yugoslavia is irrigated.
Most irrigated land is found in Macedonia and Kosovo, where the major portion
of Yugoslavia's intensive vegetable growing is undertaken. The social sector,
cultivating 35% of the cultivated land in the country, controls at least 50%
of the total irrigated area. Most irrigation systems operated by the individ-
ual farmer sector are traditional gravity systems, most of which are ineffi-
cient and antiquated.
8.        The potential for irrigation in Yugoslavia, estimated at about 3.1
million ha varies by regions, depending largely on the amount and pattern of
annual precipitation. In the Danube, Sava and Drava rivers systems irrigation
provides supplemental water needed during the months of June through September
when temperatures are high and precipitation is erratic and often low. In the
Danube-Tisa-Danube complex the need for supplemental irrigation is even more
pronounced. During the vegetative period April-October about 600 mm of water
is required. Over a 20 year period, five years have suffered a 50% shortage
of water, and five other years had a 33% shortfall in achieving the required
water supply. Irrigation in these areas when supplemented to the normal pre-
cipitation pattern could increase production by approximately 50%.
9.        The largest returns to irrigation are available in Macedonia and
Kosovo, where crop production is constrained by inadequate water supply. In
these areas irrigation makes intensive farming possible, and a full water
supply can increase crop production from 60% to 200%.
10.       The need for drainage, including both surface and subsurface drainage,
is most pronounced in Croatia, Slovenia and Montenegro where non-permeable sub-
soils prevent the realization of crop production potential. The total area
requiring drainage in the three Republics comprises about 1.2 million ha. In
some areas the land is not utilized for agricultural purposes because of an
absence of drainage. Together with land development and consolidation, drain-
age would be particularly beneficial to the individual farmer sector. In
many regions it would be a precondition for efficient mechanization.
11.       Agriculture in the Economy.  In Yugoslavia almost 35% of the popula-
tion lives in rural areas. Agriculture accounts for 16% of the social product,
constitutes roughly 8% of total commodity exports and employs about 42% of the
labor force of Yugoslavia. Though its role has been declining relatively with
rapid industrialization it still remains a sector of basic importance in the
overall development of the economy. Yugoslav agricultural policy (paras 25-30)
has passed through various phases. After the abandonment of collectivization



ANNEX 1
Page 4
in 1953, the Government concentrated on developing agricultural marketed pro-
duction through a modern social sector (incorporating the earlier state farms
and agricultural cooperatives which replaced the collective farms) as the
priority instrument for food production to meet the demand of the rapidly
growing non-agricultural population. In social sector agriculture, heavy
investments were made, to modernize and mechanize production, to increase the
use of chemical inputs and to expand irrigation. The individual farmer sector
was until recently relatively neglected by Government policy, which places
emphasis on voluntary cooperation between peasant farmers and the social sec-
tor agriculture as the major device for increasing production in the indivi-
dual farmer sector. The social product in agriculture increased at an average
rate of 2.7% per year during 1971-75, with the social sector output increasing
at about 5.6% per year and the individual farmer sector at about 1.9% per year.
In 1975, average per capita income amounts to about 41% of that of the rest of
the economy.
12.       During the last 15 years economic indicators of the role of the agri-
cultural sector have evolved as follows:
1961      1966       1971       1975
Agricultural Land as %
of Total Area                                58.3       58         57         56.6

13.       The role of agriculture in the economy of the republics varies with
the level of development, the share of agricultural land in the total area,
the quality of soils and the cropping pattern (Table 2). The share of agri-
culture in social product is relatively high for the less developed republics
like Kosovo (25%) or Macedonia (21.2%), less important for the developed
regions such as Slovenia (7%) and Croatia (13.4%) but still relatively high
in developed regions such as Vojvodina (29%) and Serbia (17.8%) where condi-
tions are favorable for high agricultural productivity and where cooperation
between the social and the individual farmer sector is effective.



ANNEX 1
Page 5
14.       Organization of the Agricultural Sector.  One of the important
characteristic features of Yugoslav agriculture is the separate existence
of two sectors i.e. social and individual with different farm structures.
The social sector has its origin in the postwar collectivization policy,
and received further impetus by the Agrarian Reform of 1953, which limited
the size of private or individual holding to 10 ha of cultivable land or 25
ha of forest land, and brought 276,000 ha of additional land under the social
sector. Since then, the land resources of the social sector have almost
doubled due to reclamation, purchase and lease from private holders. The
social sector in 1975 held 15% of the cultivated land, employed 5% of total
agricultural manpower, produced 26% of the agricultural product and 46% of
the market production. In 1975 there were 2,363 social sector holdings averag-
ing 1,063 ha of cultivated area per holding. The social sector includes (i)
agroindustrial kombinats (integrated production and processing units); (ii)
agricultural estates (both general agriculture and livestock farms); and (iii)
general agricultural cooperatives (zadrugas) with their own agricultural pro-
duction as well as provision of services to individual farmers. All these
organizations operate under the principles of social ownership of the means of
production and workers' self-management. Since 1974 to further promote the
integration of social and individual sectors the Government has developed the
concept of so-called Basic Organizations of Associated Labor (BOAL) which
integrates production, processing and marketing functions of the individual
and social sectors operating under the self-management principle implying dis-
tribution of profits and losses among the associates.
15.       The social sector absorbed more than two thirds of the gross fixed
investment in agriculture in the post war period. The capital intensity of
the investments increased from Din 1,136 (1972 prices) in 1966 to over Din
4,000 in 1975 while the number of trained workers increased by 56% and the
number of permanent workers decreased by 34%. At the same time the volume of
production in the sector increased by about 50%. The main constraint to
further development of the social sector agriculture is the lack of capital
to develop the scarce available land and to expand and modernize its outdated
processing facilities.
16.       The individual farmer sector comprises about 2.6 million small
peasant holdings of an average size of 4.0 ha. It has about 94% of total
agricultural population and 85% of cultivated land. The size of private
holdings is limited to a maximum 10 ha of arable land. However renting and
leasing is not restricted by law. The number of persons that may be employed
on a wage basis by a farmer is legally restricted to a maximum of two persons.
The productivity in the individual farmer sector is significantly lower than in
the social sector. Incomes of social sector farm workers average several times
those of individual farmers, reflecting this disparity. The social product per
worker at 1975 prices in the individual farmer sector is only 24% of the same
social product in the social sector; while the social product per hectare of
cultivated land is also only 53% of the same social production in the social
sector. Investments per ha in the private sector is only 11.7% of the invest-
ment per ha in the social sector in 1973. The main reasons for these differ-
ences are (a) lack of capital, (b) lack of credit facilities for the purchase
of better quality seeds and breeds, fertilizers and machinery, (c) lack of



ANNEX 1
Page 6
extension services, and (d) overall lower quality of land. About one-third
of the rural population is estimated to be in the relative poverty target
group with incomes per family member of less than Din 6,138 (US$341) per
year.
17.       The individual farmer and social sectors are for the most part com-
plementary rather than competitive. Labor intensive crops, such as vegetables
and tobacco, are almost exclusively produced by individual farmers, who in
addition own 97% of the sheep and 95% of the cattle herds. Cooperation between
the sectors takes the form of trade in input and output, provision of serv-
ices by the social sector such as supply of fertilizers, harvesting, cultiva-
tion, marketing and credit and the contracting of joint production. The social
sector also provides most of the extension service available in Yugoslavia.
About 30% of all smallholders cooperate with the social sector in one way or
another while 15-20% participate in contract cooperation. Contract cooperation,
in particular, is mainly concentrated in more developed regions and with rela-
tively larger peasant farmers which provide input to social sector food pro-
cessing facilities. The very small farmers, particularly in the underdeveloped
mountainous regions are least affected by such cooperation. One important
inter-relation between individual sector agriculture, and the social sector in
general exists in the supply of labor. An increasing number of peasants while
living on the farm are engaged in part-time non-agricultural employment, and
this is an important source of income for peasant households.
18.       Research, education and provision of technical services are provided
by some 50 independent institutes for research, dealing with specialized agri-
cultural problems. In addition, there are facilities for agriculture and vet-
erinary science at various universities. The institutes are financed by the
republics and the communes, and by payments for contract work done for enter-
prises. The universities are the training ground for managerial staff, and
other technical workers. In addition there are some 200 educational centers
where farm workers are trained.
19.       Marketing and Prices.  In Yugoslavia there are first the "official"
social markets for all agricultural products with authorized purchases and
sales, and then the "informal" markets based on peasant market places. The
value of authorized purchases amounts to 40-50% of total agricultural product
at present; the rest is either consumed by the agricultural population or sold
on peasant markets. There is large regional variation in the relative impor-
tance of authorized market production. Likewise, in the case of industrial
crops such as tobacco, sugarbeet, sunflower, total marketed production is more
than 70% of total production while in the case of vegetables it is only 10%.
20.       Some of the social sector enterprises have their own arrangements
for export marketing particularly in the case of processed fruit, vegetable
and wine. In order to centralize and coordinate export marketing and ensure
quality control and standardization, the Federal Government is establishing
export agencies in major European markets. Due to their smaller scale of pro-
duction individual farmers rely on the processing capabilities and marketing
facilities of the social sector enterprises.



ANNEX 1
Page 7
21.       Three types of pricing arrangements are used in Yugoslavia according
to the technical difficulties in the implementation of price policy on various
markets. These are:
(a) Guaranteed minimum prices by Government applied for all important
agricultural goods (cereals, meat, wool) for which the implementa-
tion of a price guarantee is technically possible;
(b) Minimum prices - without guarantee of the Government - prescribed
mainly for those agricultural goods which need processing such as
milk, sugarbeet and other industrial crops. These prices are
determined on the basis of the production costs in the social
sector and are agreed to in contract cooperation; and
(c) Prices free from intervention, for goods which are marketed mainly
on peasant markets or are highly perishable, so that effective
price control is practically impossible.
22.       Intervention through official pricing has not greatly affected price
distortions in produce markets because of its limited scope. In addition to
this pricing system, the Government, through federal or local budgets provides
premiums to the producers of basic commodities. These bonuses of 4-5% of the
minimum prices, are in the case of the social sector reserved to finance
investment programs.
23.       Taxes.  Social sector agricultural enterprises pay income and turn-
over taxes in much the same way as industrial enterprises. However, agri-
cultural investments and most of the inputs are exempted from turnover taxes.
The income tax is generally calculated proportionally to the income earned.
Its rates differ from republic to republic (0.8% to 13%), and are in Serbia
and Kosovo progressive to the level of income. In addition to these taxes,
the social sector contributes to various funds such as Skopje Earthquake
Reconstruction Fund, and the Fund for Development of less Developed Regions.
The basic tax obligation of the individual farmer is the land tax calculated
according to the cadastral value of the land. The valuation is determined
every five years by the local authorities and is based on the estimated
productivity of the land. The land tax rates vary between 20% in Bosnia-
Herzegovina and 9.5% in Croatia of the estimated annual income earned by
exploiting the land.
B. The Social Plans
24.       Policy Making in Agriculture.  At the federal level, coordination
of agricultural policies is achieved by the Federal Chamber of Economy, and
the Federal Committee for Agriculture. The Federal Chamber has specialist
committees to achieve coordination of research, planning, finance, etc.,
and the Committee coordinates the plans, programs and legislation of the
Republics. Responsibility for agricultural policy implementation lies with
the Republic Secretariats for Agriculture, and is coordinated at the Federal
level by the Committee. Due to decentralized decision making, all functions



ANNEX 1
Page 8
associated with policy implementation, rest with autonomous self-governing
organizations, and the Secretariats only retain indirect control via fund
disbursements. Production plans are designed and implemented by kombinats
and cooperatives or in some cases by special enterprises in accordance with
self-management principles. Even though they account for only about 30% of
total farm land the kombinats are an effective medium for policy implementa-
tion regarding production and investments, since they have a virtual monopoly
of technicians and management skills.
25.       Development Strategy and Priorities.  The overall performance of the
agricultural sector in the Plan period 1971-1975 was better than under the
previous plan. Actual growth of total production was 2.7% on average compared
to the projected 3.5%, and the 2.1% realized under the previous social plan
(Table 3). The individual farmer sector in particular lagged behind - despite
its productivity gains - realizing only 1.8% annual growth compared to 3.0%
in the Plan. This trend is the result of various aspects of investment, pro-
duction and distribution patterns which favor the social sector. In 1973 as
a result of Government discussions on agriculture development the Republics
and Autonomous Provinces approved a common Agreement on the Long-Term Develop-
ment Policy for Agriculture (Agricultural Development Plan). This Agreement
reflects the realization among political and economic authorities that increas-
ing food production requires the cooperation of small farmers. The Agricul-
tural Development Plan's basic objective is to raise production to meet ex-
pected domestic needs and allow for some exports (Table 4). It emphasizes:
(a) further development of the social sector through modernization of produc-
tion techniques, expansion of processing facilities, better utilization of
available resources and the adjustment of production to changes in demand; and
(b) development of individual farms primarily through cooperation with the
social sector. The Plan's objectives, revised in 1976, are now 4% per annum
agricultural production growth for the period 1976-1980, with the social sec-
tor expected to grow at 8% and the individual farmer sector at 3%. Within the
field of livestock production, which is projected to increase at 4% per year
(Table 5), the Plan gives high priority to cattle breeding in both the indi-
vidual and social sectors. The production of beef is largely intended for
exports, and, accordingly, quality improvement is emphasized; milk and milk
products are expected to be consumed domestically. The Plan stresses the need
for technical and organizational improvements in the collection and process-
ing of milk, particularly from the private sector. Emphasis is also given to
increased sheep production in mountainous and generally poor areas. The pro-
duction of wheat and rye is projected to develop in line with domestic need,
including the formation and maintenance of an adequate level of reserves.
Production, now about 5 million tons per year, is foreseen to increase to more
than 6 million tons by the end of the decade. Production of feed grains,
particularly corn, is planned to increase to about 13 million tons per year by
1985, to meet the growing needs of dairy and meat production. Concommitantly,
a significant expansion in storage and drying facilities will become necessary.
The production of vegetables, fruits and grapes is planned to increase in line
with rising living standards at home and increasing export possibilities. The
Mediterranean climate zone, where winter and early spring vegetables can be
grown, should provide the bulk for exports.



ANNEX 1
Page 9
26.       The Plan considers the development of efficient food processing
industries essential to agricultural development and the improvement of product
quality. With rising standards of living domestic demand is expected to shift
increasingly to higher quality and processed products.
27.       To achieve its objectives the Plan focusses on price policies,
investment policies and cooperation between the social and individual farmer
sectors. The Government intends to continue through interventionist sales and
purchases to keep market prices within 10-15% of minimum prices as agreed be-
tween the producers and the marketing enterprises. It intends also to develop
the system of premiums for basic commodities (cereals, milk, industrial crops)
to boost their production. Special incentives are foreseen to encourage mobil-
ization of enterprises' own resources, such as income tax exemption for invest-
ments in agriculture; turnover tax exemption and interest rate subsidies for
investment credits to agriculture; reduction to import duties on agroindustry
equipment not produced in Yugoslavia; elimination of Dinar deposit require-
ments 1/ with the National Bank for foreign credits channelled into agricul-
tural investments; and increase in the foreign exchange retention quota for
depreciation charges 2/. On the basis of preliminary discussions within Gov-
ernment at Federal and Republic levels it is expected that the total invest-
ment for the Plan period would amount to Din 75 billion (US$4.2 billion) in
1975 prices of which the social sector would invest Din 53 billion and the
individual farmer sector Din 22 billion. About 20% of the required financing
is expected to be provided by enterprises or investors' own resources, 40% to
be provided by Government and 40% by the Banking system of which about 10-20%
are from foreign sources. Government intends to expand cooperation among
farmers and between farmers and social sector organizations to increase produc-
tivity in the individual farmer sector. Such cooperation would enhance modern-
ization of small farms and mobilize more resources for investments in that sec-
tor. The Republics and Provinces have agreed to support cooperative arrange-
ments through provision of financing, and interest rate and tax policies.
They also agreed to pass legislation that would allow cooperating farmers to
join the health and social insurance pension schemes of social sector organiza-
tions.
28.       The Agricultural Development Plan, for the first time in Yugoslavia's
postwar development, provides a comprehensive guideline for the development of
agriculture, encompassing both the social and the individual farmer sector. In
recognizing the potential of the individual sector and explicitly supporting
its development, the Plan is a big step towards better resource utilization
and more even income distribution. However, in limiting its support only to
farmers that either form a cooperative or cooperate in some way with the social
1/   Enterprises and banks borrowing abroad are required to deposit a certain
percentage of the foreign credit in dinars with the National Bank with-
out earning interest.
2/   Enterprises are entitled to retain in foreign exchange 10% of the annual
depreciation charges.



ANNEX 1
Page 10
sector, the Plan will not reach a substantial portion of the agricultural popu-
lation, notably the smaller farmers and those in the mountainous and infertile
areas.
29.       The Plan's projected growth rate for the social sector (8%) seems
to be optimistic as the Plan fails to recognize that the declining growth
rates in the sector since 1965-66 is not accidental. It indicates a change
in the growth pattern since the social sector with the land and technology
available at present is approaching the level of development where production
increases become more difficult. Because of the two limiting factors growth
of primary production in the social sector is unlikely to exceed 4% per annum
in the next five years. On the other hand the planned annual growth rate of
3% for the individual farmer sector seems achievable. It implies, however, a
growing income discrepancy between the sectors unless the labor force in the
individual farmer sector decreases by about 5%. It is unlikely that migration
of such magnitude would take place. Individual farmers would continue to seek
to increase of their incomes through off-farm employment and renting of addi-
tional land to increase their farm size.
