YUGOSLAVIA
FINANCIAL AND INSTITUTIONAL ASPECTS
A. Legal Framework of Banks in Yugoslavia
General Provisions
1.        The basic legal framework under which Yugoslav banks operate is
incorporated in the Federal Banking Law of December 1971 and in each bank's
statutes. M4ajor elements include several classic cooperative principles
such as:
(a) Each member of a bank is called a founder and has one vote
in the Founders' Assembly, regardless of the size of con-
tribution to the bank's resources;
(b) The founders of the bank have unlimited liability for the
obligations of their bank;
(c)  Statutory regulations pertaining to joining a bank are
minimal, and under specified procedures a founder may
withdraw from a bank (an event unlikely to occur frequently
in practice).
Other provisions of the Law enable Yugoslav banks to engage in a wide range of
transactions and activities along the usual commercial banking lines, and also
include provisions which specify that:
(d)  Founders may include only "social legal persons", i.e. social
sector enterprises.   The minimum number of founders is 25,
and their combined total contribution to the Founders' Fund
must equal at least Din. 50 million (US$2.78 million);
(e) The bank's statutes prescribe the powers of the various managing
organs. A bank's highest governing body is the Assembly con-
sisting of representatives of its founders;
(f) The Assembly appoints the bank's Chief Executive and members
of the Executive Board. The Chief Executive is responsible
for the implementation of Assembly and Executive Board
decisions. The Executive Board is responsible to the
Assembly for policy formulation (subject to Assembly
approval), for oversight of operations and for certain
operating decisions not delegated to the Chief Executive;



ANNEX 2
Page 2
(g) The Assembly approves the bank's annual business policy
(budget) and the policy on interest rates and decides on
the allocation of income to reserves and the distribution
(in aggregate) to the bank's working community (staff)
and founders;
(h) The founders of a bank are typically also founders of a
branch of their bank. Branches have their own Founders'
Assembly and a management structure similar to that existing
for a bank as a whole. All founders of branches are also
founders of a bank as a whole;
(i) Banks are required to make a yearly appropriation to a Reserve
Fund out of income, of at least 0.5% of loans outstanding at
the year-end, until the fund amounts to 5% of loans outstanding.
This fund can be used only for writing-off bad debts and, tem-
porarily, for meeting the bank's obligations in circumstances
of tight liquidity. The resources of this fund have to be
generally maintained in liquid or semi-liquid funds;
(j) Banks are required to write-off bad debts first against income.
Write-offs against the Reserve Fund are only possible to the
extent that there is insufficient income;
(k) A bank may be liquidated if it is unable to meet its obligations
for more than six months;
(1) In case of liquidation, the deposits of individuals have priority
over all other creditors of the bank. (Deposits of individuals
are also guaranteed by the Federal Government under a separate
law).
2.        In addition to the Banking Law, the banks have to comply with
various regulations and directives issued by the National Bank of Yugoslavia
and the Federal Government. The main directives which have a direct implica-
tions on the bank's financial operations are:
(a) Liquidity Requirements. The banks have to maintain a certain
proportion of their short term deposits as statutory reserves
with the National Bank, evidenced by non-negotiable cashier's
notes of the National Bank. (The interbank clearing function
is handled by the Social Accounting Service (SDK). The rate
varies for different classes of deposits, and the average
level has been in the 10-25% range for participating banks
between 1973 and 1975. Also, banks are required to keep
nominal minimum cash positions.
(b) Foreign Exchange Regime. All foreign borrowings are
regulated by the National Bank and have to be registered
with it prior to and after the borrowing is contracted.
Only banks specially authorized by the National Bank can
undertake foreign exchange transactions.



ANNEX 2
Page 3
(c) Limitations Regarding Banks' Overall Commitments. Under a
Federal law, passed in the second half of 1975, a bank is
required to keep the level of its liquid investment poten-
tial which is determined from a bank's balance sheet and
cash flow projections (paras 5 and 7). Under the law,
the Yugoslav Federal Executive Council specifies which
resources constitute a bank's liquid investment potential
and the conditions under which guarantees can be made.
3.        To summarize these observations concerning the legal and regulatory
framework of commercial banking in Yugoslavia, the main points are that the
banks are organized according to several classic cooperative principles; con-
duct a range of transactions and activities within the conventional scope of
commercial banking; have a nanagement structure similar to that of coopera-
tives and corporations; have a relationship with the central bank similar to
that found in many other European countries, except that the central bank
appears to have relatively less influence over interest rates; and are bound
by statutory formula regarding the accumulation of a Reserve Fund and the
writing-off of bad debts.
B. Concepts of Bank Management and Accounting
4.        As suggasted by the cooperative basis of bank ownership, the banks
are ideally not supposed to act independently of the requirements of their
founders. In fact, a bank's founders are also its largest borrowers, and
banks may be perceived as financial vehicles of mutual assistance among
founders as well as a means by which founders gain access to the savings and
working balances of citizens held in the form of bank deposits. In addition,
banks act as loan windows for certain funds allocated for priority development
purposes by fiscal authorities at the republic (republic funds) or provincial
levels (provincial funds), and at the federal level (federal funds) with re-
spect to the four less developed regions. Banks also manage funds on a trustee
basis for various organs of the state, such as subdivisions.
5.        In addition to its statutes, each bank operates according to an
annual "Business Policy" which implies preparing, against the background of
the Five-Year Plan, a program statement about the Bank's main objectives,
magnitude of operations and interest rates structure. The planning of bank
lending programs is ideally derived from the aggregated investment plans and
working capital requirements of founders and undertaken on a five year basis
coinciding with planning periods. However, this ideal is achieved only to
varying degrees, and the procedure sometimes used in fact involves an annual
plan derived from applying some inflator to recent levels of performance.
The primary emphasis in banks' planning is not so much on outstanding or
risk exposure as on disbursements. The relative unconcern for exposure
reflects the view that the banks are viewed as an integral part of the
economy (defined to include "productive" sectors only) and are the financial
arm of their founders, which are also the major borrowers. Lending is un-
secured, but supported by founders' unlimited liability and by efforts to



ANNEX 2
Page 4
ensure the technical and financial feasibility of the loan-supported invest-
ment. The disbursement approach to planning is engendered by the perception
of banks primarily as channels of funds for productive purposes rather than
as intermediaries serving society by altering the risk and term structure of
the accumulation of financial claims of economic units which create a finan-
cial surplus. Planning variables of key importance consist of projections of
disbursement potential based on a bank's cash flow expectations. Separate
calculations of disbursements potential are made for long term and for short
term needs. Variables are adjusted monthly on the basis of results and new
developments. The system appears in general to work satisfactorily, providing
coordination between the investment plans of enterprises and availability of
resources according to priorities established by the Green Plan.
6.        Bank managemert is based upon the concept of matching sources and
uses of funds. The application of this concept in Yugoslavia involves a
number of features which tend to de-emphasize the fungibility of finance and
to restrict the independent growth and specialization of the financial sector,
in accordance with Yugoslav economic philosophy. The matching concept is also
used to foster decentralization of decision making by stressing matching at
the lowest possible level of the kombinat by means of an internal bank (para
14), which permits the maximum possible matching of internal sources and uses
of funds. Kombinats join together to form a bank, thereby pooling their
financial balance and requirements, and also tapping the pool of citizens'
private financial resources in the vicinity of the founders' production
activities. The tradition of branch autonomy in banking also reflects this
philosophy. The organizational framework emphasizes the matching of sources
and uses at the branch level, for the branch's founders and with the local
community, rather than viewing the branches largely as devices for securing
resources and locating lending opportunities for the head office or for the
bank as a whole.   Entire banks may also join together to form associated
banks (para. 16) for specified purposes. The relationship between one level
and the next in these pooling hierarchies is in principal based on the net
financial position of the lower level, or on special services which are most
easily provided at a higher level, such as Eurocurrency borrowing. The
Yugoslav approach to matching is also seen in the use of earmarked deposits,
which may be used by banks for lending only in some specified manner.
7.        At the level of internal planning within a bank, matching principles
are followed on a temporal basis. The short term credit potential is defined
as a function primarily of the behavior of short term balance sheet items.
Likewise, the liquid investment potential which is applied to the longer term
is derived largely from the projected behavior of long term assets as well as
of certain specified short term items. A former emphasis on matching short
term assets and short term liabilities on a one-to-one basis has been somewhat
relaxed, but banks are supposed to use at least 20% of their short term re-
sources for the satisfaction of borrowers' seasonal or other short term working
capital requirements not exceeding three months in length, and to provide at
least another 15% of their short term resources for financing the permanent
working capital requirements of borrowers in loans extended for up to two years.
In addition, loans to individuals for consumption and certain other purposes
and to enterprises for the financing of retail trade are viewed as being



ANNEX 2
Page 5
financed by the deposits of individuals, with an emphasis on not permitting
advances of the types specified to exceed the resource base provided by
citizens' deposits. Ideally, matching of this type is effected on the level
of each branch.
8.        On a day to day basis banks manage their liquidity positions on four
different levels of activity. The first is the coordination of the positions
of individual branches, which is one of the benefits perceived to flow from
the strategy of concentration in banking. The 25 banks which existed in
Yugoslavia in 1973 traced their period of decentralization and specialization
which began in 1955. The extreme decentralization reflected in the existence
of more than 200 banks was ended by a movement towards concentration to
strengthen the banking system and to create larger banks to serve larger
borrowers, among other goals. The second level of liquidity management is
the interbank short term funds market where transactions are conducted which
have a tenor rarely exceeding 30 days. This market operates between large
banks and also through the Association of Banks, a type of trade association,
and also there are no externally imposed restrictions on interbank indebted-
ness. The market appears to operate without the benefit of dealers and
trading accounts by principle, and hence only matches bona fide primary sur-
plus and deficit positions. The third is through recourse to the National
Bank. However, the use of this means of maintaining liquidity is discouraged
by the rigorous terms - a penal interest rate and the limitation on borrowings
to ten working days a month, applied to branches on a republic or provincial
basis. The fourth level of adjustments available for liquidity management is
the curtailing of long term lending, which in times of tightness is accorded
lower priority than the maintenance of short term disbursement programs. It
should be noted that the Reserve Fund of each Yugoslav bank must be kept in the
form of cash or in the form of obligations of the National Bank or the Federal
Republic.
9.        Certain aspects of Yugoslav bank management are difficult to pene-
trate, and these relate to the planning mechanism or process in Yugoslavia.
It is not known on exactly what basis founders agree on the allocation of
resources in each bank's annual plan. Another area which is unclear is the
extent to which the management of a branch or a bank takes initiatives in
matters of resource allocation. Complete control nominally rests exclusively
with the founders, but is however, substantially shared with bank management,
which with its accumulated expertise exercises a certain amount of independent
initiative in day to day operations. The nature and scope of this initiative
is difficult to assess. But proposed changes in the banking legislation
suggest that the tendency for banks to develop a life of their own exists and
is regarded as excessive.
Accounting Conventions and Information
10.       Bank accounting in Yugoslavia is substantially different from
that generally accepted in Western countries. The major differences in the
Yugoslav system and the difficulties in reconciling these differences with
Western presentations are summarized below:



ANNEX 2
Page 6
(a) The branches of the banks prepare their own balance sheets
(including net worth accounts) and income statements. In
the preparation of statements for a bank as a whole, the
individual balance sheet items of its branches are often
combined without any consolidating entries to eliminate
interbranch transactions;
(b) Bank financial statements include funds they manage at no
risk on a commission basis on behalf of local authorities
(socio-political organizations). These are not included,
however, in the financial data supplied by the banks to IBRD;
(c) Short term loans are defined as having maturities of up to
two years. Data on maturities of less than two years on
loans of original tenor exceeding two years is not aggre-
gated, and it is therefore impossible to separate current
and long term positions strictly according to normal account-
ing definitions;
(d) Accounts are maintained for each loan classified by purpose
and/or source of funds (e.g. loans for fixed assets or for
specific working capital items, and loans from Federal funds,
republic funds, etc.). A borrower may have numerous loans
outstanding, and no routine record is presently kept by a
bank of its total exposure to any one borrower. All a bank's
accounts are maintained by the Social Accounting Service;
(e) The banks observe strict confidentiality as regards financial
information received from their clients. They cannot disclose
a client's financial data without the consent of the client
concerned;
(f) Banks do not generally attempt to make a periodic assessment
of potential losses within their loan portfolios. Annual
appropriations to the Reserve Fund can be considered as
provisions against losses, but are not the result of a
case-by-case review of their loans outstanding;
(g) The appropriation for reserves is not treated as an expense
item in determining the bank's net income. The banks, how-
ever, are required by law to write-off, against income, 25%
of their total uncollected interest overdue by 45 days or
more at the end of a year.
Audits
11.       The Social Accounting Service (SDK) is responsible for the audit of
all enterprises, including the banks, in terms of Yugoslav law. The audit is
mostly in the form of a certification that the banks' and enterprises' opera-
tions are within the various legal requirements, that the statements are in



ANNEX 2
Page 7
agreement with the records kept by the banks and correct charges of interest,
allocation of net income, etc. have been made. There is no verification
of the existence of the various assets and liabilities to the banks and no
opinion on whether they are fairly treated. In addition to the SDK, the
banks are subject to inspections by the National Bank of Yugoslavia, which
ensures, from time to time, that the banks comply with the regulations on
foreign-exchange transactions and special deposit requirements.
12.       Normal audit by SDK does not conform to internationally accepted
standards, but SDK has implemented a training program for its staff, with the
Bank's support, to undertake internationally accepted audits of Yugoslav banks.
Full audits for all PBs will not become available until after the end of the
disbursement period of the Project, given the number of banks involved in
SDK's program and the difficulties in verification posed by the scope of the
banks' operations and the absence of conventional auditing by international
standards at the level of enterprises which borrow from the banks.
The Proposed Banking Law
13.       During the disbursement period of the Project the Yugoslav banking
system is expected to experience a reorganization. The draft banking law
which has been under discussion in Yugoslavia and which is expected to be
enacted in a form quite close to that contained in the draft, has as its
object the greater integration of the banks with the needs of their founders.
The law is consistent with the Yugoslav view that financial resources should
be controlled directly by the workers who use these resources in the produc-
tion process, and with the M4arxist dogma that these resources arise from
or that their existence is made possible by the contribution of labor as
a factor of production. Implementation of the law should not change any
of the fundamental principles of financial philosophy in Yugoslavia--on
the contrary, it should increase the scope of their application.
14.       With respect to form, the law recasts the banking structure into
three tiers. The first tier is the internal bank (of which several are already
in existence) which in its most common form will be in effect a centralized
financial information unit and internal payments clearing center in a kombinat.
The liquidity positions of the organizations in each kombinat are to be moni-
tored centrally by the kombinat's internal bank. Through the monitoring
system, surpluses and deficits in cash positions of constituent units will be
netted out and borrowing and repayment arrangements will be coordinated. The
aspects of self-finance promoted by the formation of internal banks are rela-
tively innovative in the Yugoslav context. An internal bank is not in a
position to create credit, but merely acts as a financial service center for
the kombinat which formed it. As such, the contribution of internal banks is
expected to be the strengthening of the organizations they serve. Internal
banks are expected to be empowered to operate savings accounts for workers and
also to mobilize citizens' savings for specific purposes related to the produc-
tion of the kombinat the internal bank serves.
15.       The second tier of the banking structure will consist of basic banks
which will be organized on a local basis to serve the needs of a small number



ANNEX 2
Page 8
of founder kombinats or basic organizations of associated labor (Annex 1, para.
19) and the community of workers and others in the immediate vicinity of the
production activities of the founders. Basic banks will conform to the pattern
of retail commercial banks as conventionally perceived and be the only compre-
hensive type of banking organization. However, basic banks will tend to be
unit operations or consist of only a small number of branches.
16.       The third tier of the banking structure will be composed of asso-
ciated banks which will consist of at least three basic banks united for
specified purposes. Basic banks will delegate certain functions to the asso-
ciated bank to which they belong, such as foreign borrowing (for the account
of a specific borrower only), arranging syndications and cofinancing, and
undertaking or coordinating feasibility studies, etc. Associated banks will
also serve their founder basic banks by facilitating the flow of resources
between founders in a manner similar to that seen in the activities of in-
ternal banks, at the kombinat level.
17.       Implementation of the new banking legislation will occur slowly.
The constraints of staff and the expertise required to establish internal
banks will ensure that the presently limited number of such banks will expand
at only a moderate pace. Likewise, the head offices of the present business
banks (commercial banks), such as those that participate in the Project, will
be transformed into associated banks, and it will require time to reorganize
and decentralize the present structure into associated and basic banks 1/.
In fact, the portion of the proposed legislation relating to associated banks
is scheduled for discussion in 1977, and it is unlikely that major organiza-
tional changes will be fully implemented before 1978.
18.       With respect to substance, the banks' role is seen as shifting more
towards that of financial service units. Credit decision-making will be decen-
tralized to the basic bank level to the extent possible. Founders will retain
greater direct control over their working balances and surplus funds through
the mechanism of the internal bank and through the advice of earmarked deposits
with basic banks, allocated for specific purposes only. Within basic bank
decision making structures, the role of Credit Committees having a composition
including bank management, will be de-emphasized while the role of Executive
Committees composed of representatives of founders will be enhanced. While
the rationalization of financial resource use implied by these structural
changes would appear to remove deposits from basic and associated banks, the
bankers interviewed indicated that a decline in deposits would be unlikely
to occur because of the growth of the economy, the slow rate of formation
projected for internal banks and because of the importance of the deposits
of the individuals in the banks' resources.
1/   Udruzena Banka Zagreb, a participating bank, was founded and begun
operations in 1972 as an associated bank, and reportedly served as
a prototype for the proposed legislation.



ANNEX 2
Page 9
C. The Agricultural Credit System in Yugoslavia
Credit to the Social Sector
19.       The social sector is defined to consist entirely of social sector
enterprises, many of which are agroindustrial kombinats which are vertically
integrated from farms through retail outlets and cooperatives (Annex 1, paras
18-19). Finance for investment by social sector enterprises comes from three
main sources:
(a) self finance by the investor;
(b) debt from a variety of sources, including trade credit,
suppliers credits, the business banks of which the bor-
rowers are usually founders, loans from special funds
operated by Federal, Republic and Provincial authorities,
and direct finance from other kombinats; and
(c) minor sources, including Government grants.
20.       Statistics pertaining to investment by social sector agroindustrial
kombinats and including the entire range of their investments, some of which
may not be related to agriculture because of the conglomerated nature of
certain kombinats, indicate that self finance and debt have each supported
between approximately 40% and 50% of investments in fixed assets over the
Plan periods extending from 1966 to 1975.
Plan Period      Investment Expenditures         Self-      Debt    Grants and
for Fixed Assets          Finance                  Misc.
1966-1970         Din 10,611 million             39%        49%         12%
1971-1975         Din 24,310 million             48%        43%          9%
Large scale investments in new facilities typically involve a lower participa-
tion of self-finance than suggested by the aggregated figures above. Rela-
tively large outlays for plant and equipment and working capital rely heavily
on debt. It is estimated that roughly 80% of the investment undertaken in
social sector agriculture and agroindustry over the 1966-1975 planning periods
was financed by sources external to the investing kombinats. Published sta-
tistics do not permit a precise specification of debt according to type or
source, but brief summaries indicate the scope and conditions attached to
major sources.
21.       Trade credit has been a financial pressure point in the Yugoslav
economy, as suggested by the implementation of a law in March 1976 specifying
standard payment terms between enterprises. Cash shortages manifest them-
selves in the accumulation of accounts payable, which then spread throughout
the economy with trade between production units. Such accumulations result
in many enterprises' being unable to meet their obligations to domestic trade
creditors in a timely manner. This is the most general characteristic of



ANNEX 2
Page 10
the liquidity problem. The legislation put into effect in 1976 provided
for the netting out of positions of firms and acceleration of the rate of
settlement of trade debts to a uniform period of about 15 days. However,
the statute also permitted settlement via 90 day bills of exchange, and
since the introddction of the new regime there has been a general movement
towards the adoptioa of this hitherto relatively little used means of
settlement.
22.       Suppliers credits are widely used in transactions involving the
acquisition of capital requirement from abroad. Users of suppliers credits
may be required to make dinar deposits at the National Bank for a certain
portion of the value of the credits obtained. In cases requiring such
deposits, the proportion ranges up to 25%, depending upon the type of goods
obtained, the usance period, and whether the Yugoslavian principal to the
transaction is located in a developed or less developed region.
23.       Banks provide loans to their founders in accordance with the in-
vestment plans of their founders, which are included in the enterprise plans
which constitute, inter alia, the basis for economic planning in Yugoslavia;
and also in accordance with the banks' own positions. Most borrowers which
are social legal persons are also founders of the bank from which they borrow,
and a few large borrowers may be founders of several banks. Agrobusiness
finance is also provided by special funds organized at the federal, republic
and provincial levels. These funds are designed to support investment in
the developed areas, while in the less developed regions the "Federation Fund
for Crediting Development of Economically Insufficiently Developed Republics
and Provinces", hereafter referred to as the Federal Fund, predominates.
24.       The Federal Fund has been constituted largely by subscriptions
by enterprises in the form of obligatory loans on terms of 15 years at 4%.
Federal budgetary sources were also tapped to meet a Din 2.4 billion short-
fall in Fund resources during the 1971-1975 period, and it is envisaged that
enterprises may also contribute to the Fund as investors according to self-
management agreements. The Fund places its resources at the disposal of
business banks in three of the four less developed areas on terms of 14 years
at 4.166% with a grace period of three years, and in Kosovo at 17-1/2 years
at 3%, also with a grace period of three years. The banks on-lend these funds
for specified purposes on slightly more rigorous terms, and are liable for re-
payment regardless of the fortunes of the investments financed.
25.       The Federal Fund is expected to disburse more than Din 50 billion
(in 1975 Dinars), equal to approximately 2% of the social product, during the
1976-1980 planning period. It will finance roughly 30% of all investments
in fixed assets (agriculture and others) in the beneficiary regions, varying
from a projected 16% in Bosnia and Herzegovina, 24% in Macedonia, 28% in
Montenegro to 60% in Kosovo.
Credit for Individual Sector Farmers
26.       An estimated 80% of Yugoslavia's primary production in the agricul-
tural sector occurs on individual farms. While a significant portion of this



ANNEX 2
Page II
production is consumed on the farm and sold privately to consumers in local
markets by the producers themselves, the remainder is sold to social sector
units engaged in food processing and marketing. Accordingly, the links be-
tween these social sector units and the individual producers are key elements
in the food economy. As under the First Agricultural Credit Project, a
project goal is to strengthen the links between the agroindustrial sector
and primary producers in the individual farmer sector.
27.       Credit is one vehicle for strengthening these links.  Institutional
credit is in general not available to individual farmers as individuals, but
only to them on the basis of their affiliation with a cooperative or a social
sector enterprise. Credit may be extended to individual farmers by a kombinat
as part of an agreement to cooperate or marketing contract, whereby the farmer
agrees to deliver all of his produce of a specified type to a social sector
agroindustrial processing firm at a price which is specified at the start
of the season. Contracts of this type generally involve a commitment of
the farmer to cooperate for a five year period, although the contractual
obligation is fixed for one year only.
28.       Individual farmers find it useful to cooperate for several reasons.
These include their limited access to markets without the assistance of an
intermediary, which is a social sector unit. In addition, their incomes are
constrained by reliance on self-finance, and credit is largely unavailable,
except through cooperation with the social sector.
29.       The social sector units find it useful to contract with individual
producers to secure sources of supply to supplement primary production in
the social sector itself, to realize economies of scale in processing and
retailing, to obtain a larger flow of raw materials to justify increased
capital investment and hopefully increased returns per worker, and to in-
crease their degree of penetration in the market for processed products.
30.       Social sector units are frequently dependent upon their banks for
the resources required to finance loans to farmers for increases in herds
and for the acquisition of capital equipment and the construction of build-
ings. This dependence reflects the general liquidity problem. Cooperation
between the individual farmer and social sectors has probably been limited
by the shortage of funds facing many social sector units, which appears to
have resulted in a rationing of funds in favor of investment in the social
sector for primary production and for processing, rather than in strengthen-
ing the links between the social agroindustrial sector and the individual
agricultural sector. In addition, these links have remained relatively under-
developed because the skills required to organize the necessary marketing
mechanisms and qualifications found in agroindustry which are largely tech-
nical and oriented towards engineering and agriculture.
31.       In addition, the establishment of sound links requires the social
sector purchaser to be able to perform consistently in terms of accepting
delivery and ensuring prompt payment. This type of consistency is probably
easiest to achieve in the case of products with an almost continuous produc-
tion cycle, such as dairying. It appears most difficult to achieve in the
cases of products which have a short season, and short shelf life, such as



ANNEX 2
Page 12
early vegetables, and which are consequently subject to large production
and price variation.
D. Proiect Implementation Capacity
Performance Under the First Agricultural Credit Project
32.       The institutional structure of the proposed Project is identical
to that developed and utilized under the First Agricultural Credit Project,
and the risks of non-performance and implementational difficulties are there-
fore very substantially reduced. The lead bank role is filled by Vojvodjanska
Banka (VB), with its head office in Novi Sad, Vojvodina Province.
33.       VB's Business Policy and other decisions of its Founders Assembly
guide its operations each year. Investment proposals that enterprises submit
to VB for financing are appraised and approved in a branch office, or in the
head office, depending on the size or complexity of the loans. VB's exposure
or the exposure of any of its branches to a single client should not exceed
30% of total outstanding loans, and no one borrower accounts for more than
10% of its total portfolio. VB operates as a mixed commercial/investment
bank and is authorized to deal in foreign exchange. Supervision of loans,
with regard to borrowers' financial positions and procurement arrangements,
is satisfactory, but could usefully be expanded to include more areas of
dialogue with borrowers. Disbursement procedures are sound. The other par-
ticipating banks include Privredna Banka Sarajevo (PBS) in Bosnia-Herzegovina,
Udruzena Banka Zagreb (UBZ) in Croatia, Kosovka Banka Pristina (KBP) in Kosovo
Province, Stopanska Banka Skopje (SBS) in Macedonia. Investiciona Banka
Titograd (IBT) in Montenegro, Beogradska Banka (BB) in Serbia Proper, and
Ljubljanska Banka (LB) in Slovenia. These banks are with one exception
classified as business banks and are among the top 15 in terms of size in
Yugoslavia. The exception is UBZ, which is an associated bank. Its own
staff consists of fewer than 30, yet it is classified as the largest bank in
Yugoslavia based on the size of the combined resources of the five Croatian
business banks which are its founders.
34.       Performance under the First Project has been generally satisfactory
and the pace of implementation has accelerated over its life. Effectiveness
occurred on February 12, 1976, and by March 1977 about 50% of the US$50 mil-
lion IBRD Loan under the Project had been committed by the participating
banks. The entire Loan is expected to be fully committed in the second half
of 1977, about only 18 months following effectiveness. Completion, targeted
for June 30, 1979, will probably be substantially achieved on schedule.
35.       Experience under the First Project has demonstrated that the least
difficulty is experienced in implementation after several conditions were ful-
filled. The first is that the responsibility for implementation be squarely
placed in both the participating bank and also in the Ministry of Agriculture
in the republic or province concerned. At the participating bank level this
has generally meant the formation of a special unit to deal with subproject



ANNEX 2
Page 13
appraisals or else the strengthening of any agricultural unit which may al-
ready be operative. Implementational responsibility has been effectively
carried out only in those areas in which subproject preparation has been
handled out of participating banks' head offices, and by the final quarter
in 1976 all of the participating banks had made satisfactory arrangements
in this regard. The subproject appraisal requirements of the First Project
placed extraordinary demands upon participating banks relative to conven-
tional loan appraisal techniques for the agricultural and agroindustrial
sectors, necessitating these developments. At the level of provincial and
repdblic ministries of agriculture, the First Project also required substan-
tial efforts at coordination. Senior officials, usually advisors and under-
secretaries, have been given specific responsibilities for the First Project
and their role in promoting subproject identification and preparation at the
level of social sector subborrowers has been essential to project success.
36.       A second condition for successful implementation has been enthusiasm
for the Project at the federal level, with monitoring to secure the achieve-
ment of commitment targets. The Federal Committee for Agriculture has been
the focal point for Project affairs coordination and promotion, which have
required a major portion of the efforts of the Undersecretary of the Federal
Committee and of certain members of his staff.
37.       A third condition for successful implementation has been the desig-
nation of a lead bank, which is Vojvodjanska Banka. While it was envisaged
by IBRD that the lead bank would exert very strong influence by acting as a
step in the loan approval process, the modus operandi which has developed is
one of VB's making a strong coordination to the preparation of subprojects
by the prior training and briefing of those in borrowing kombinats and in
participating banks who are directly involved in putting together subproject
proposals. This system has demonstrated its viability under Yugoslav con-
ditions, and VB's role would be continued and expanded under the Second
Project. VB's inputs have included training seminars and discussions, and
the exchange of information on an informal basis with participating banks
and subborrowers, as well as the more narrowly administrative tasks involved
in its leadership role.
The Role of Vojvodjanska Banka as Lead Bank
38.       Although a small bank--it ranks sixth in portfolio size among the
eight banks involved--VB is the most highly specialized in agricultural lend-
ing in terms of the proportion of its portfolio outstanding to agriculture.
Precise figures for comparison are difficult to derive because records of ac-
tivities are kept largely on a disbursement rather than on a loan outstanding
basis, but it would appear that more than 25% of VB's portfolio is outstand-
ing to agriculture and to agroindustry, while none of the other participating
banks would have an exposure of this sort in excess of 15%.
39.       Project implementation is the responsibility of a Project Unit con-
sisting of 16 established posts and headed by a Director who has an organiza-
tional status similar to that of an Assistant General Manager, of which VB
has only four (see the organization charts of VB and of the Project Unit in



ANNEX 2
Page 14
Appendix 1). As of December 1976 four of the 16 Project Unit posts remained
vacant, although two of these positions were for loan supervision officers
whose responsibilities had not yet been phased in because the evolution of
the project cycle had not yet generated many subloans to supervise. Other
vacant posts included that of Advisor and that of Senior Officer--Agricultural
Credit. At the time of appraisal efforts were underway to fill these posi-
tions.
40.       Under the proposed Project the Project Unit will be expanded by the
addition of a procurement specialist, who will be responsible for assisting
participating banks and their borrowers under the project in the procurement
of goods and services locally and internationally. The procurement specialist
will become involved in agroindustrial subproject proposals at an early stage
in their development and supervise all aspects of such investments and their
appraisal which relate to procurement. He will also assist in the prepara-
tion of tender and related documents to be submitted to IBRD at stages prior
to the presentation of Loan withdrawal applications.
41.       The Project Unit consists of two Divisions.  One is concerned with
subproject appraisal, while the other is concerned with "implementation",
defined to include disbursements, records, legal aspects and supervision.
Both Divisions are headed by senior operating personnel with considerable
experience in VB and are staffed with qualified specialists largely in mid-
career. It is not expected that the nature or role of the Project Unit will
be changed for the Second Project. Some additional established posts may be
required, however, especially on the "implementation" side, as the volume
of loans outstanding increases.
42.       The Second Project will provide an opportunity for VB to expand its
activities in acquainting an increasing number of economists with the sub-
project appraisal methodology recommended by IBRD. The economists at whom
orientation and training programs should be aimed are found mainly in par-
ticipating banks, in kombinats which are in a position to receive Project
funds, and in ministries of agriculture. VB's responsibility for formal and
informal training inputs should be supported by IBRD in the form of EDI places
at an early stage in the life of the Project and by assistance at training
seminars in the form of material, and perhaps also through inputs from visit-
ing missions, as under the First Project.
E. Financial Characteristics of Vojvodjanska Banka
and Participating Banks
General
43.       Financial data supplied by the eight participating banks is sum-
marized in Tables 1-5. For each bank balance sheet and income statement data



ANNEX 2
Page 15
are given for the years ending from 1973 to 1975 1/. The data should be
interpreted with caution, especially in those cases in which the material
was provided by the banks themselves rather than by the Social Accounting
Service team which has begun partial audits of some of the banks according to
generally accepted accounting standards. Some of the financial data are com-
bined from the statements for each of the branches of a bank, which probably
introduces the most serious distortion by failing to eliminate inter-branch
items. Others may not be presented on a consistent basis by failing to pro-
vide restatements of past data to adjust for mergers which occurred over the
period. Missing data for 1973 for one bank reportedly reflects the failure
of that bank's recording system to accommodate the effects of a merger which
occurred during that year, and this type of extreme situation indicates that
caution is required when dealing with financial data supplied by Yugoslav
banks. Even the data provided in the conventional Western format by the
Social Accounting Service auditing team does not carry an unqualified opinion
by the auditors "because of the limitation of scope" of their examinatibn,
resulting largely from difficulties in verifying data with the bank's clients.
It will be several years before full audits and unqualified opinions can be
considered.
44.       Projections for the years 1976-1980 are included in each table.
These should be viewed within the context in which they were generated. Not
all of the projections supplied by the banks balanced, some contained mathe-
matical errors, and none contained reconciliations of net worth. Where pro-
jections prepared by participating banks fell short of conventional Western
bookkeeping standards, the data presented includes considerable input by
IBRD staff. Growth assumptions generally appear modest relative to recent
rates of growth reported by the banks concerned, and officials of several
banks indicated they expected bank assets to expand at the same rate at which
the Material Project for their Republic or Province was planned to expand
over the planning period through 1980.
45.       Analysis of the data provided gives a general indication of the
financial nature of banking in Yugoslavia, as reflected in the financial struc-
ture and performance of the eight banks. In addition, analysis permits the
identification of some interesting differences among these banks which relates
to the different types of regional economies they serve and the differing man-
ner in which they cater to the needs of their founders. The assumption em-
ployed throughout the analysis is that the data are presented consistently
and give a true valuation of the conditions they portray or project.
Financial Analysis of Vojvodjanska Banka
46.       Size and Capital Structure.  Annual financial statements for the
period 1973-75 indicate that VB is a growing institution. In current Dinar
terms, the compound annual growth rate in total assets over the 24 month
1/   Earlier data from the First Agricultural Credit Project Appraisal Report
is not repeated here. Several banks appear to have altered their report-
ing basis between the preparation of this and the earlier Project report.



ANNEX 2
Page 16
period was almost 32% (item x.01 in Table 1), which even after adjusting for
inflation at about 24% in 1974 and 18% in 1975 represents significant expan-
sion. This growth has occurred more rapidly in liability than in capital
accounts, with the result that the Capital to Assets Ratio 1/ declined from
12.5% to 9.1% over the period (x.23). More than half of VB's liabilities
(2.20) are at short term (2.05) which is defined to include original maturi-
ties at less than two years, and their relative importance grew between 1973
and 1975. Sight deposits (2.02) comprise the largest class of short-term
liabilities. Loans from Banks and Other Sources (including IBRD) (2.11) con-
stitute the largest single class of Long Term Liabilities (2.15) contributing
almost half of this term category, while Time Deposits (2.10) and Loans from
Provincial Funds (2.12) provide the balance.
47.       Asset Structure and Liquidity.  Almost 90% of VB's assets consist
of financial assets (1.30). The proportion of loans outstanding extended at
short term (less than two years) (1.04) has increased dramatically over the
period under review as suggested by a rise in the Banking Asset Structure
Ratio 2/ from 40.8% to 48.2% (x.53). However, both short term (1.04) and
long term (more than two years) loans outstanding (1.13) have increased sig-
nificantly in Dinar terms. The level of liquidity maintained relative to
1/   Capital to Assets Ratio.  Assets are defined as total assets.  Capital is
defined as Net Worth including the Founder's Fund, Reserve Fund, Business
Fund and other funds which will be categorized as Funds of the Bank as a
Working Organization following the enactment of the new Banking Law in
1977. One of the difficulties which arises in dealing with Net Worth
in the context of Yugoslav banking is that certain of the funds are
earmarked for specific purposes and therefore may not serve as a general
reservoir of protection for bank's creditors. Another is that contribu-
tions to the Founder's Fund are not permanent and distributions from
Founders' funds have reduced the size of this account by small amounts
from one period to the next in several cases among the eight banks
included in the Project.
2/   Banking Assets Structure Ratio.  This measure, which expresses Short-Term
Risk Assets as a percentage of Total Banking Assets, measures the degree
to which a bank is "commercial", i.e. the extent to which its banking
assets are lent at short term. A high ratio shows a greater emphasis on
a short-term lending, a loan ratio a lesser emphasis. Most of the banks
consider short term maturities to extend to two years. As records are
kept on the basis of original term to maturity the banks could not
specify the current portion of their long term lending. Hence, the
ratio conservatively overstates the maturity structure of a bank's
portfolio. On the other hand, certain facilities extended at short
term are in fact permanent sources of finance for borrowers in spite
of having a turnover within the short term.



ANNEX 2
Page 17
deposit liabilities is small, as indicated by Deposit Run-Off Ratios 1/ of
less than 1% (x.37). Most of primary reserves consist of statutory reserves
held with the National Bank (1.02). Liquidity is assisted by certain types
of Short-Term Borrowings (2.01) which funded approximately 10% of Total Bank-
ing Assets (1.30) as shown on year-end balance sheets for 1973 through 1975.
48.        Income Analysis.  Income Statements show that financial expenses
(item 5.30 in Table 2) consume about 70% of VB's Total Operating Income (5.10),
although the level of these expenses fell to 66.6% in 1975. Administrative
Expenses (5.50) trended upwards over the period from 10.9% to 11.8% of income,
with Salaries and Related Expenses (5.41) rising at a more rapid rate than the
growth of income. The before-tax Net Operating Surplus (5.70) stood at ap-
proximately 20% of Total Operating Income (5.10) over the period, with the
best results achieved in 1975. The Return on Total Assets trended upwards
from 1.0% to 1.2% over the period (x.83). Viewed from the cooperative basis
of Yugoslav banking, it is not possible to say whether these measures reflect
changes in operating efficiency, as the provision of better services to
founders might be viewed as salutory even if its impact on the income state-
ment were adverse. However, it is apparent that the operations of VB over
the period yielded an increasing Return on Net Worth (x.73). In terms of
this criteria VB was substantially more profitable in 1975 than in 1973, as
the rate of return rose from 7.6% to 13.4%. Of even greater interest is the
fact that the Return to Net Worth is projected to rise to 22.5%, three times
the 1973 level, by 1980, which leads to a review of the projected financial
statements.
49.        Financial Projections Analysis.  The financial analysis submitted
by VB show ony minor variations from straight line and constant proportion
assumptions, with the following important exceptions. The rate of growth in
Total Assets (1.50) is expected to decline from a level of about 35% in 1975
and 1976 to about 15% per annum by 1980. The decline is tied to planning
1/   Deposit Run-Off Ratio.  This ratio shows the percentage decline in total
deposits which could be met from Minimum Risk Assets alone.  The ratio
gives an indication of the extent to which a bank can rely on its own
Minimum Risk Assets rather than using short-term borrowing or other
sources of funds to meet deposit withdrawals. The higher the ratio
the greater the ability of the bank to meet such demands from its prim-
ary reserves of liquidity, which consist of Cash and Banks, and Deposits
with the National Bank. This ratio is computed by an algebraic method
which assumes deposits withdrawals would be met by the total depletion
of Cash and Banks plus that portion of Deposits with the National Bank
which would be released by a decline in deposits. A simplifying assump-
tion is that withdrawals would affect all classes of deposit liabilities
proportionately. Any actual withdrawals would probably affect different
classes unevenly, with a resulting uneven release of statutory reserves
because of the different reserve requirements specified for different
types of deposits. The assumption that banks' deposits with the
National Bank include only statutory reserves and that excess reserves
are not carried is largely justified in Yugoslav conditions.



ANNEX 2
Page 18
targets in Vojvodina and to expectations of a lower rate of inflation. The
role of capital is expected to be de-emphasized, as suggested by a decline in
the Capital to Assets Ratio (x.20) from 9.1% in 1975 to 5.2% in 1980, which
is part of the explanation for the steady growth of the Return to Net Worth
(x.73). Liquidity is expected to increase markedly with increases in primary
reserves in spite of an expected decline in the proportion of total deposits
absorbed by statutory reserve requirements (x.33). However, liquidity is still
projected to remain at low levels. The Deposit Run-Off Ratio (x.37) is ex-
pected to be only 1.5% in 1980, after a jump from 0.6% in 1975 to 1.2% in 1976.
The loan portfolio is expected to show a slightly increased emphasis on the
expansion of long term rather than short term lending, which is consistent
with VB's role as lead bank under the Project.
Comparative Analysis of Participating Banks
50.       Balance Sheet Relationships.  Tables 4 and 5 show the values of cer-
tain key variables from participating banks' financial statements. Between
1973 and 1975 most of the banks grew rapidly in dinar terms. Four increased
their total assets by more than 35% during 1975. In almost all cases deposit
liabilities grew faster than total assets during this period, with deposit
growth rates exceeding 50% per year recorded in certain cases. The growth of
bank capital failed to keep pace with overall expansion, and Capital to Asset
Ratios declined from a mean level of roughly 9.7% to a mean of about 7.8%.
Other ratios likewise indicate a general tightening of financial relationships,
reflecting the demands of rapid expansion a well as the possibility of increased
efficiency in banking. Liquidity, as suggested by the deposit run-off ratio,
declined, with the greatest decreases registered in 1974, a year in which
credit was reportedly tight. In conformity with the decline in the Capital
to Assets Ratio, the cushion against portfolio illiquidity, as indicated by
the Portfolio Shrinkage Ratio 1/, also declined. With these changes, banks
probably tended to move slightly towards shorter maturities in their lending
portfolios, as suggested by the increase in the Banking Asset Structure Ratio.
This movement is also consistent with the efforts then underway to work down
the backlog of trade credit in the economy, which would have tended to in-
crease the demand for short term relative to long term credit.
1/   Portfolio Shrinkage Ratio.  This ratio is computed by decucting Minimum
Risk Assets (primary or liquidity reserves) from both Total Banking
Assets and Total Liabilities and dividing the residual on the asset
side by the residual on the liability side. One minus the resulting
percentage is the Portfolio Shrinkage Ratio, which measures the maxi-
mum possible percentage loss in Banking Assets other than Minimum Risk
Assets which would permit the liquidation of all liabilities. The
higher the ratio the larger the loss in value of its loan portfolio
a bank could sustain and still remain technically able to meet the
claims of its creditors from its portfolio.



ANNEX 2
Page 19
51.       With respect to the types of intermediation provided by the various
banks, negative Term Transformation Ratios 1/ indicate that some of the smaller
banks tend to borrow long and lend short. This reflects their dependence upon
Federal Funds resources. The relatively high liquidity positions of several
of the smaller banks, as seen in Deposit Run-Off Ratios, are indicative of
difficulties encountered in disbursing these funds for investment at the same
rate at which these funds were received. However, the relative liquidity
position of these banks also tended to become somewhat smaller over the
period, indicating that the backlog of Federal Funds may be diminishing in
relative terms. The banks in developed areas and Banka Privredna Sarajevo
tend to follow the normal patterr of borrowing short and lending long.
52.       It appears that the trend towards expansion and increasing tight-
ness in key financial relationships is expected to continue through 1980.
Growth in Total Assets is expected at a more moderate rate than experienced
between 1973 and 1975. The tendency for the smaller banks to grow the most
rapidly in relative terms is expected to be less pronounced by 1980. Deposit
Liabilities are projected to continue to expand more rapidly than other lia-
bilities and net worth taken as a whole. Net Worth will play an increasingly
minor role as a basis for expansion and as a cushion for creditors, which is
consistent with the tone of the proposed banking legislation which is intended
to diminish the potential for independent action by the banks and tie them
more closely to the financial flows of their founders.
53.       The pattern of lending is expected to change slightly in favor of
relatively more short term lending in the smaller banks, as indicated by the
increasing banking Asset Structure Ratio, while the relationship between short
term and long term lending is expected to remain fairly stable in the larger
banks. The Term Transformation Ratio exhibits a mixed pattern among the banks.
Some expect to increase their dependence upon short term resources for financ-
ing long-term lending, while others expect little change or a decrease in such
dependence.
54.       The projections indicate that the banking system will be increasingly
tight by 1980, with lower margins to accommodate unexpected events. Whether
the system can operate on such a basis while continuing to meet the needs of
its founders and the public at the same level provided during 1973-1975 de-
pends upon several factors. If the system is more efficient, it should be
able to operate on a tighter basis. If the system has more recourse to the
central bank or to outside sources of liquidity, it should also be able to
operate on a tighter basis. In addition, lower margins would be justified
by better integration of the Yugoslav economy if such integration resulted
1/   Term Transformation Ratio.  This ratio is a measure of the extent to which
a bank borrows short and lends long. One of the main risks of banking
arises from intermediating between those having financial resources
placed at sight and those borrowing for a specified period. This ratio
measures term transformation by subtracting Short Term Risk Assets from
Current Liabilities and dividing the difference by Short Term Liabilities.



ANNEX 2
Page 20
in the reduction of risk and in smoother financial flows through the banking
systems.
55.       Income Statement Analysis.  During the period 1973-1975 the par-
ticipating banks experienced increases in Total Operating Income of between
8% and 47% annually, with rates above 25% being normal. While in 1974 Total
Operating Income grew more rapidly than Total Assets, in 1975 the opposite
situation was the most common. Projected Total Operating Income is expected
to grow somewhat less rapidly in the period through 1980, and generally at a
declining rate of increase. This prediction is consistent with the cooperative
basis of Yugoslav banking under which the founders who are also the major bor-
rowers attempt to keep the costs of borrowing low, preferring cheap credit to
distributions of earnings from their investment in captive banks.
56.       Financial Expenses consumed between 55% and 84% of the total operat-
ing income of participating banks on an annual basis between 1973 and 1975,
with rates of 60% to 75% being the most common. Financial Expenses as a per-
centage of Total Operating Income tended to decline over the period, and this
trend is expected to flatten out in most cases through 1980 at levels of be-
tween 65% and 75%. The pattern with respect to Administrative Expenses as a
proportion of Total Operating Income is more varied between banks for the
period 1973-1975, and participating banks projecting these costs expect them
to be fairly stable at between roughly 8% and 17% through 1980. Annual Admin-
strative Expenses equal approximately 1% or less of Total Assets of most par-
ticipating banks, are projected to remain at roughly this level, and appear
to be well controlled.
57.       Net Operating Surplus as a percentage of total operating income
ranged from between 9% and 26% over the 1973-1975 period, and is projected to
remain stable for most of the eight banks through 1980 at levels commensurate
with those achieved in 1973-1975.  However, one of the banks expects the per-
centage to decline to 2% by 1980 as efforts are undertaken to increase person-
nel costs and to pare costs borne by borrowers.
58.       The returns on Total Assets were confined to the 0.5% to 1.5% range
during the 1973-1975 period, although this range is expected to expand slightly
at both ends by 1980. During the 1973-1975 period returns on Total Assets
trended upward for most of the banks for most of the annual periods involved,
whereas for the period 1976-1980 the banks project constant or declining
returns. Most of the expected declines are small, however.
59.       Returns on Net Worth also increased markedly in most cases between
1973 and 1975, with the highest six cases ranging from 5.3% to 12.0% in 1973
and between 11.9% and 17.7% in 1975. This increase is related to the decline
in Capital to Asset Ratios over the period. While Capital to Asset Ratios are
expected to continue to decline between 1976 and 1980, the projected levels of
returns to net worth are varied--several banks expect significant increases in
this measure of profitability (up to a level exceeding 22%), several expect
relative stability in this relationship (at between 11% and 18%), while others
project declines (from 11% down to 2%). The lack of agreement on the develop-
ment of returns to net worth over the period to 1980 indicates varying



ANNEX 2
Page 21
strategies relating to the pricing of bank services to founders and to the
public, as well as somewhat varying prescriptions of the role of bank capital
within the general consensus that its financial importance will decline.
Interest Rate Strategy in the Banking System
60.       Interest rate levels appear to be associated with bank size.  Finan-
cial data for seven of the eight participating banks was submitted on a more
or less consistent basis, and these figures indicate that bigger banks tend
to charge higher average rates and also to pay more for their resources than
do smaller banks. Graph 1 shows both the average lending and borrowing rates
for 1975, computed from income statement and balance sheet information pro-
vided by the seven banks. The positions of five of the seven show virtually
a linear correlation between rates and portfolio and liability totals as shown
by visually-fitted trend lines in the graphs. The two exceptions to this rela-
tionship are the two smaller banks. Their positions are associated with their
heavy reliance on Federal Funds resources and their high levels of liquidity
(as indicated by deposit run-off ratios) which may lead them to behave dif-
ferently as regards growth strategies than the other banks not having such
preferred access. The causality, if any, of the association between interest
rate levels and bank size is not apparent from financial data, and definitive
analysis would require more resources than available for financial aspects
of project appraisal. Several alternative hypotheses may be specified, how-
ever. One is that the association is fortuitous. Another hypothesis is that
banks grow by offering more attractive interest rates to depositors, and that
founders are willing to pay more for the services of banks which can provide
them with the benefits of access to a larger pool of funds. This hypothesis
is supported by the data in Graph 2 which plots administrative costs against
average total assets and includes a visually fitted trend line. Larger banks
appear to be relatively more costly to operate than smaller banks, and there
must be some advantage in scale which offsets this diseconomy.  The possibil-
ity that larger banks have better access to more profitable lending opportuni-
ties is diminished by the fact that the asset side of Yugoslav banking is
tied to the financial demands of founders. Perhaps advantages of scale in
founders' industries create a situation in which founders' access to greater
quantities of financing are reflected in founders' enhanced ability to
supply resources to their captive banks. Possibly, the exploitation of such
economies by founders is associated with a willingness to pay more for finan-
cial resources, to incur higher administrative costs in their captive banks,
and to see higher rates of interest paid to households and others who are in
a position to provide funds to banks. The existence of an illiquidity problem
in recent Yugoslav financial history would provide a rational basis for com-
petition for funds by banks, and for the use of interest rates as one aspect
of such competition.
61.       Another hypothesis is that the average interest rates merely reflect
the structure of assets and liabilities. On the asset side, banks with rela-
tively more short term lending business (generally at 10-12%) will generally
have a higher average lending rate than those with relatively more long term
loans (generally at 3-12%) and those with relatively more loans to individuals
(generally at 8-12 1/2%) will have a higher average rate than banks lending

relatively more to kombinats (generally at 3-12%), other things remaining
equal. On the liability side, rates on the sight and time deposits of indi-
viduals (generally at 7 1/2% and 9-10%, respectively) are higher than those
paid on deposits held by kombinats (generally 2-4% on sight and 4-10% on time
deposits). Also, banks with heavier degrees of reliance on short term borrow-
ing, which occurs at uncontrolled rates in the interbank market, will incur
greater costs for funds than banks with smaller levels of such reliance.
62.       These observations fail to explain why or how a bank's business
evolves in one direction rather than another. Does emphasis on short term
lending and retail banking constitute a better growth strategy than long term
lending and wholesale banking? Quite possibly it does, given the illiquidity
problem and the role of the household sector as a source of funds for the
social sector. Whatever its explanatory power, this hypothesis is useful
in that it points out the shortcomings of analysis incorporating assumptions
of homogeneity in the sources and uses of bank funds.
63.       An institutional approach to analysis would consist of ascertaining
how the banks came to be the sizes they are, given their evolution out of the
amalgamations of more than 200 predecessor institutions. In this respect it
should be noted that the largest bank, Udruzena Banka Zagreb, is an associated
bank. Its five constituent banks, ranging in size from extremely small to an
institution of roughly the same size as Privredna Banka Sarajevo and Stopanska
Banka Skopje, should perhaps constitute the units of analysis if consistency
is desired. However, this observation begs the question of why these five
banks, apparently with high cost and interest rate levels, associated to form
UBZ. If a smaller bank, such as Vojvodjanska Banka, were to grow through
merger or association, would its average lending and borrowing rates automati-
cally rise?
64.       In summary, bank size measured in terms of loan portfolios, total
banking assets and total liabilities appears in general to be positively asso-
ciated with the relative levels of interest income earned and interest expense
incurred by the seven participating banks for which data was provided on a
largely consistent basis. The two banks which are an exception to the general
trend are also exceptional in terms of their high liquidity, suggesting that
liquidity may offer a useful approach to analysis. Elementary manipulations of
financial data show that all of the banks have a positive interest rate spread,
that the spread tends to increase with the size of the bank. However, analysis
at this level does not provide definitive explanations of the relationship ob-
served, and this aspect of the role of interest rates in Yugoslav banking can-
not be defined with precision.
Interest Rate Policies
65.       Interest rates in Yugoslavia are characterized by their frequently
low levels, ranging from 3% to 12% on bank loans, for example, and by their
lack of uniformity across internal geographical boundaries, among banks, and
for similar types of investments. This situation raises a number of issues
which relate to the role of interest rates in economic and financial decision



ANNEX 2
Page 24
making in Yugoslavia. They influence resource allocation through the selec-
tion of investment alternatives, and the choice of technique. Interest rates
also influence resource mobilization through selection of one financial asset
rather than another by savers or those with working balances to invest, the
levels of accumulation at various levels of investment priority, and numerous
other factors related to both allocation and mobilization. Recent interest
levels in Yugoslavia have fueled excessive investment demand and contributed
to inflation.
66.       To ascertain more fully the function of interest rates in the
Yugoslav context a study was commissioned under the First Project. The terms
of reference of the study were to analyze the interest rate structure for the
agricultural sector and to determine whether uniform interest rates would be
effective and feasible. The study was to consist of three parts:
(a) an evaluation of the impact of the existing agricultural
credit interest rates structure,
(b) a review of other factors affecting the interest rate struc-
ture, such as Government policies, the banking structure and
viewpoints of agricultural social sector enterprises and of
private farmers;
(c) in the light of the findings of parts (a) and (b), a deter-
mination of whether a uniform interest rate system is both
desirable and feasible.
67.       The study was contracted by Vojvodjanska Banka to the Institute for
Agricultural Economics in Belgrade. Their conclusions 1/ were available late
in 1976, and these indicate (a) that within the Yugoslav socio-political
system, characterized by socially owned means of production, workers' self-
management and decentralized decision-making, economic subjects independently
decide on basic issues of their activity including the volume and structure
of production, prices, size and structure of investments and financial transac-
tions; (b) that the market mechanism is essential in determining the decision
making on these matters; and (c) that individual plans of enterprises and
socio-political communities are subsequently coordinated and adjusted into a
consistent overall development plan for individual regions and the Federation
through a process of discussions and negotiations. While stressing the role
of the market in decision-making, the study does not analyze the effect of
interrelationships between market forces and institutional and political
factors on the prices. The study concludes however that heavy price distor-
tions exist. These distortions reflect a relatively broad, direct inter-
vention by the state which reduces the allocative role of the market in
general as well as of interest rate in particular.
1/   "Effects of the Interest Rate on Long-Term Agricultural Credits--Policy
of Interest Rates in General and in Agriculture in Particular," Institute
for Agricultural Economics, Belgrade, December 1976.



ANNEX 2
Page 25
68.       Review of the investment decision making process by the Appraisal
mission confirms that interest rates in Yugoslavia indeed play a role in
investment decision making at the investors' level. Interest rates are among
a number of influences which determine resource mobilization and allocation
patterns. Other influences include political and institutional factors related
mainly to the structure of the banking system, the planning process and pricing
policies at enterprise, republican and federal levels, and measures which
determine the extent to which financial resources are mobile across internal
political boundaries. Because of (a) the preceding factors and their complex
interaction, but also because of the lack of a clear statement or understand-
ing of their extent; and (b) the decentralization at the financial institutions
and/or republican levels at which interest rates are determined, interest
rates may not be used efficiently when viewed against Yugoslav economic goals.
They should be kept at levels which reflect the scarcity of capital, which
promote the mobilization of resources, and which cover the costs of lending by
banks. In addition, interest rate distortions resulting from the variations
in rates between areas, banks, investments and sectors should be eliminated
to the extent they are counter-productive in terms of planning priorities.
The method of financing investments in agriculture and agroindustry usually
involves a blending of budgetary (special funds) sources and of other sources
mobilized through banks. Budgetary sources are usually provided on conces-
sional terms, so that the effect of blending is to decrease the overall in-
terest cost to the borrowers.
69.       The study on interest rates commissioned under the First Project
does not provide a sufficient basis for deciding upon an optimal interest
rate policy in Yugoslavia. Further work remains to be done. The future study
should arrive at recommendations which would constitute the basis for a coor-
dinated policy on interest rates.  In view of the current initiative of the
Association of Yugoslav Banks to undertake a study on interest rates in
Yugoslavia, and their willingness to solicit the Bank for assistance in this
respect, it is recommended to provide the Association with the proposed terms
of reference in Appendix 2 of this Annex and request the Government and the
Association to take all appropriate action for launching an interest rate
study as soon as possible. It is further proposed under the Second Agricul-
tural Credit Project that in addition to setting the minimum rate of interest
for on-lending Bank funds at 11% per annum, floor rates of interest for the
local funds lent under the Project be instituted. While the extension of
these rates to all similar lending undertaken by the PBs would serve to
enhance the effectiveness of the Project lending, it is appreciated that
such an extension may not be appropriate until the results of the specific
study efforts outlined above are known.
70.       The rates to be applied for local funds employed under the Project
could be the blended cost to agricultural or agroindustrial borrowers of funds
provided by the banks. Assuming a blend of 50% from Federal funds or special
funds and 50% from other resources of PBs the prescribed minimal rate would
be 6% as applied to social sector subborrowers and a 5% blended rate as applied
to individual sector subborrowers. These rates are expected to ensure the
banks with a minimum return from their lending operations in agriculture of
about 2.5-3.0% on Bank funds and about 1.2% on local funds portion of their
subloans.
