ANNEX 2
Appendix 2
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Interest Rate Study
Draft Terms of Reference
Objective
1.        In the framework of the First Agricultural Credit Project (World
Bank Loan 1129-YU), Vojvodjanska Banka entrusted the Institute of Agricultural
Economics, Beograd, to undertake a study to analyze the interest rate struc-
ture for the agricultural sector, and its broad economic implications.
2.        The report of the Institute which was issued in February 1977 on
"Effects of Interest Rates on Long-Term Agricultural Credits" constitutes a
useful background document on the financial and institutional structure of
the Yugoslav economy, the structure of interest rates within and between
Republics and Provinces, and the pricing and fiscal system in the agriculture
sector. This report represents by its character the first phase of a more
comprehensive study to be undertaken on interest rates and financial charges
applied by the banks in Yugoslavia. This study would cover Bank lending for
all sectors with particular emphasis on agriculture and industry. This new
study should (a) determine the role and the effect of interest rates and
other financial charges on the financial situation of the banking system;
(b) review the role of interest rates in the Yugoslav economy in general;
and (c) draft recommendations for a long-term policy on interest rates for
all banks in Yugoslavia.
Organization
3.        The study which would be financed by the Association of Yugoslav
Banks, would be supervised and coordinated by a Board whose members would
be selected by relevant governmental and financial institutions. The firm
or firms of consultants to carry out the study would be selected on the
basis of a review of the proposals received from a number of firms consulted
and interested in participating in this project. The proposals should in-
clude, in addition to a detailed information on qualification and experience
of the staff to be employed for the task, an elaborated description of the
methodology proposed to analyze the specific issues raised below.
4.        The Final Report on the study is expected to be completed within
twelve months after signature of the contract.



ANNEX 2
Appendix .2
Page 2
The Study
5.        The selected consultants would, with the assistance of the Associa-
tion of Yugoslav Banks and of Federal, Republican and Provincial government
authorities, undertake the following tasks:
A.   Review the Effect of the Interest Rate on the Financial
Situation of the Banking System
(i) Consultants will review the effects of interest rates
on the term structure of bank assets and liabilities,
with special reference to the mobilization and allo-
cation of resources at long maturities and the im-
plications of the term structure of interest rates
on the liquidity position of banks. They will review
the cost of capital to the banks as well as financial,
administrative and other costs of lending which in-
fluence the quality, the volume, and the efficiency
of financial intermediation. This review would involve
a detailed analysis, over say a five-year period, of
banking system resources, according to their sources,
costs and terms, and the banks' uses of these resources
by purpose, costs and the interest rates and other
charges or compensation received for them. In study-
ing these factors, the consultants will discuss the
various aspects of interest rate formation, and par-
ticularly assess the level of coordination among the
banks in setting interest rates and criteria used by
them for such determination.
(ii) The consultants will review the lending policies and
procedures of the banks and establish the extent to
which these policies and procedures are applied. This
empirical review of historical trends should, however,
take into account the recent institutional and legal
changes in the Yugoslav banking system. While Social
Plans and Social Compacts seem to have key importance
in resource allocation, the lending procedures and
policies of the banks may also have a role to play.
This will be studied along with the effect of interest
rates on the resource allocation process and on capital
intensity of the investments. For example, the consul-
tants will determine the extent to which credits for
similar purposes from the same Bank are lent at dif-
ferent rates and how these differentials are determined.
They will also review the extent of flexibility that the
banks have in allocating their funds between different
types of agricultural or industrial activities. Simi-
larly, the role of the investors or the factors intervening



ANNEX 2
Appendix 2
Page 3
in investment decisions and choice of technology will
be investigated. An empirical approach through case
studies might be used for this analysis.
B.   Review of the Effect of the Interest Rate on the Economy
(i) The study will determine the factors outside the banking
system which are involved in interest rates formation and
structure. Among these, the respective role of the Fed-
eration, Republics, communes and any other institutions
in subsidizing interest rates, and the rationale and
the criteria for such subsidies will be examined. The
justification for subsidies will be established to in-
dicate whether subsidies are prerequisite for adequate
financial returns to the borrowers or are necessary for
income redistribution among producers. These alterna-
tives should be tested through calculations of rates of
return, empirical review of comparative income levels
in economic sectors, and the income effect of subsidies.
The conclusion of this review will discuss whether in-
terest rate subsidies are an effective and equitable
and efficient means of overcoming other distortions.
It will also indicate whether subsidization achieves
its objectives of income distribution or cost reduc-
tion without creating other distortions on prices,
level of production, type of production, etc. It
will identify particular areas relating to long-term
interest rates for agriculture and industry, where
removing any distortions would yield significant
benefits, and examine whether major distortions in
other markets, such as the exchange rate or taxes,
should be corrected as a matter of first priority.
(ii)  The study will analyze the factors affecting savings
and examine empirically the past relationship with
interest rates; separate out as far as possible the
savings of enterprises and the savings of households
and within households savings due to workers' remit-
tances and savings held in foreign exchange accounts.
Some reference might be appropriate here to the rela-
tion of interest rates and inventories.
(iii) The consultants will also establish whether low interest
rates result in or contribute to capital-intensive in-
vestments, either through adoption of an excessively
capital intensive technology or choice of activities
with higher capital intensities which would not take
place without low interest rates. A detailed case
study approach in all the republics and provinces



ANNEX 2
Appendix 2
Page 4
could be carried out to determine the principal factors
affecting capital intensity and the extent to which the
current choice of technology is appropriate.
(iv) The resource allocation between sectors and within
sectors will be reviewed. In the agriculture and indus-
trial sectors the study will determine the extent to
which funds available are predetermined through Social
Compacts and Self-management Agreements, and further-
more to what extent these funds are earmaked for
specific types of activity. It will examine the cur-
rent situation with regard to the demand and supply
of long-term credit, and assess the rationing devices
adopted by the Plan and the financial institutions for
rationing credit to specific investment projects.
C.   Draft Recommendation for a Coordinated Interest Rate Policy
in Yugoslavia
In the light of the findings in A and B above, the consultants
will propose a set of short- and medium-term measures to be
undertaken by appropriate financial institutions and govern-
mental authorities for realization of a coordinated interest
rate policy in Yugoslavia. This policy might include uniform-
ity of interest rate for all banks in Yugoslavia.



ANNEX 3
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Fruit and Vegetable Production Component
1.        Fruit and vegetables have traditionally been primarily produced
by individual sector farmers in Yugoslavia (92%). Because of low productivity
in the sector as a result of limited investment and low level and poor qual-
ity of farm inputs, the production has been stagnant despite the increasing
demand during the last ten years. The Agricultural Plan objective is to boost
annual fruit and vegetable production by about 3.7 and 8% respectively during
the next five years. The Project would help achieve this objective by invest-
ing over a three year period US$62.3 million (27% of the total Project cost)
in fruit and vegetable, vineyard and land development. The projected invest-
ment, income and cash flow statements for five representative farm models are
shown in detail in Appendixes 1 to 5 of this Annex.
Farm Models                                   Location              Appendix
4-ha Farm with 0.1 ha Plastic House        Only Macedonia               1
4-ha Farm with 1.0 ha irrigated            All Republics except
Mixed Vegetables                         Slovenia and Croatia         2
Land Development (5 ha)                    Slovenia and Croatia         3
4-ha Farm with 1 ha Vineyard               All Republics except
Slovenia, Montenegro
and Serbia                  4
5-ha Farm with 1.0 ha Cherries             All Republics except
Slovenia, Montenegro
and Kosovo                  5
2.        Vegetable production is an important activity on individual farms.
It is a dominant cash crop in areas where markets are readily available
near the cities and tourist resort areas. The Project is expected to pro-
duce at full development about 50,000 metric tons of fresh vegetables in-
cluding mainly tomatoes, green beans, pepper and onions. About 2,100 tons
of this production would be early vegetable, mainly tomatoes and cucumbers
produced in 40 ha of plastic "walk-in" tunnels to be financed in Macedonia.
About 2,600 farmers would participate in the Project and would be investing
in construction of plastic houses, purchase of irrigation equipment, motor
cultivators, and other farm improvements.



ANNEX 3
Page 2
3.        Investment in fruit production would include development of roughly
5,000 ha of new orchards, and 1,500 ha of vineyards of which 50% would be for
replacement of old vineyards. About 6,500 farmers would be participating in
the development of fruit production under the Project. At full development
incremental production of fruit is expected to reach about 45,000 metric tons,
of cherries, apples, peaches, apricots and plums and 14,000 metric tons of
wine and table grapes.
4.        A breakdown of Project cost, number of hectares, list of major in-
vestment items and volume of incremental productions is presented in Table 1.
Prices, yields and other technical coefficients used in the models are shown
in Table 2. The yields assumptions are conservative, because of high risks in
fruit and vegetable production. Input and output prices are based on December
1976 prices. They are expected to change over time, but are assumed to change
proportionally at the same rate. Model calculations are therefore made in
constant prices. A physical contingency of 5% has been included in the invest-
ment cost to take into account differences in subprojects design and location.
As individual farmers pay taxes according to the cadastral value of their
agricultural land (Annex 1, para 23), these taxes are the same before and
after the Project. They are therefore not taken into account in the financial
analysis of the subprojects. Hired labor has been valued at the prevailing
wage rate for unskilled labor. Family labor has not been costed because of
under employment among individual farmers. The respective financial rates of
return for the proposed investments as well as sensitivity tests applied to
them, are summarized below:

YUGOSLAVIA
Representative Models
Farm Model No. 1: 4 ha Individual Farm with 0.1 Plastic House
1.        The plastic houses would be financed in Macedonia.  The proposed
model represents a farm of 4 ha in an area where the climate conditions are
favorable for early vegetable production and where the produce can be sold
20-30 days earlier than in the northern part of Yugoslavia. The farmer invest-
ment would consist in: construction of 2-5 plastic houses covering a total
area of 1,000 m2, the purchase of 60 m of irrigation pipes to deliver water
from existing water supply to the plastic house, and a 6.8-HP cultivator with
implements. The tunnel shaped plastic houses would be constructed from pre-
fabricated portable aluminum tubing.
2.        Before Project 3.0 ha of land are cultivated under cereals, and 0.5
ha are cultivated under tomatoes and pepper. The remaining 0.5 ha are under
low yielding meadow. The farmer uses his own animal power and additionally
required tractor services are supplied by a nearby kombinat, which also sup-
plies the combine for wheat harvest. Pre-Project income would be Din 28,416
per farm. With the Project the farmer would slightly increase the area under
cereal, he would maintain the area under field vegetables except 1,000 m2 for
the plastic house and the low yielding meadow would be eliminated. The farmer
would concentrate on the production of early tomatoes and cucumbers in the
plastic house and would introduce green beans, and tomato seedlings as a
second crop. All of his produce would normally be sold on the local market.
3.        The total investment cost would amount to Din 108,500 and would
yield a financial rate of return of 26% after 10 years period. At full devel-
opment (Year 3) the net farm income (before loan repayment) would be Din
54,931. About 400 of such investments would be financed under the Project
in Macedonia.

YUGOSLAVIA
Farm Model No. 2: 4 ha Individual Farm with 1.0 ha Mixed Vegetables and
Small Scale Sprinkler Irrigation
1.        The model represents an average farm of 4 ha without livestock pro-
duction and where one ha of mixed vegetables farming is to take place under
irrigation. Before Project 2.5 ha are cultivated under cereals, 0.5 ha under
grapes and 0.7 ha under vegetables, the remaining 0.3 ha consists of low
yielding meadow. The farmer grows mainly tomatoes, beans, pepper and onions.
Under the present situation, the farmer still uses one horse for land prepara-
tion. The wheat is however harvested with a rented combine from a nearby
kombinat.
2.        The farmer investment would consist in construction of a 3-10 m deep
well, the purchase of a small scale portable sprinkler irrigation system and
the purchase of a 6.8-HP cultivator with implements. The sprinkler system
would consist of a 3-HP electrical motor pump, 240 m of portable aluminum
pipes and 4 sprinklers with accessories to facilitate the irrigation of gen-
erally small and irregular fields. The system would operate approximately
1,500 hours per year and the water requirement would be about 6 m3/hour.
Model development would be limited to areas with sufficient water supply.
3.        As a result of these investments, especially the introduction of
the cultivator and better timed weed control and soil preparation, it is
expected that the yields of wheat, maize and grapes would increase by 5-15%
at full development. About 10 hours of tractor services would still be
supplied by the kombinat which would also harvest the wheat product. The
total investment would amount to Din 94.840, and yield a financial rate of
return of about 24% after 15 years amortization period. The net farm income
(before loan repayment) would increase from Din 33,700 before Project to
Din 55,820 with Project. The Project would finance about 2,200 farms in
all Yugoslavia except the republics of Slovenia and Serbia.

APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Farm Model: Individual Farmer Sector Land Development (5 ha)
1.        This model represents a farm where about 3 ha of wheat, maize and
sunflower and 1.5 ha of low yielding pasture are cultivated, but where 0.5 ha
are uncultivated because of insufficient drainage. The farm land is divided
into several small parcels, some of them with insufficient access.
2.        The investment under this model would include brush clearing, land
leveling, functional drainage and access roads.  The investments would also
include surveying of the plots to establish boundaries, machinery services
and consolidation of small parcels into larger plots to increase production
through better land utilization and mechanization. With the Project the
presently uncultivated 0.5 ha of land, with 1.0 ha of low yielding meadows,
would be reclaimed and would be transformed into more productive pasture.
The area under sunflower would be doubled, silo maize and tobacco would be
introduced as new crops. When the farm would reach its full development,
in the fourth year after investments are made, the annual farm income would
increase by about 120% from Din 11,120 to Din 24,820. The construction period
of land reclamation components of the model would be phased over a two year
period. The Project would provide for financing of about 750 ha of such land
in Slovenia and 4,750 ha in Croatia. The financial rate of return of the
investment over a period of 15 years is estimated to be 22%.
3.        The disbursement under this component would be made either in the
framework of individual investment made by the farmer, or in the framework
of land development scheme undertaken by a social sector enterprise. The
Bank funds would finance private sector share of these investments prorated
to their share of total acreage in the scheme.

YUGOSLAVIA
Farm Model No. 4: 4 ha Individual Farm with 1.0 ha Vineyard
1.        This model describes the development of one hectare of new vineyard
within a 4.0 ha farm including 2.5 ha cereals, 1 ha vegetables, fruits and
meadow and 0.5 ha of an old outgrown vineyard. The investment in the pre-
sented model would consist in clearing the 0.5 ha old vineyard and planting
1.0 ha of new grapes whereby the additional land would come from the area
under creals and the low yielding meadow.
2.        The investment would include machinery service, construction of
trellises and the purchase of a 6.8-HP motor cultivator with plow, trailer,
tiller and sprayer.
3.        The introduction of good quality rootstock and modern techniques
would improve yields from about 4 ton/ha at present to 12 ton/ha in the
future. These yields are moderate in comparison to actual obtained yields
in newly developed vineyards in the individual farmer sector. Farmers could
also be grouped to develop larger units (5-10 ha blocks) and instead of each
farmer purchasing his own motor cultivator 3-4 farmers would purchase in such
case one 35-HP tractor and its implements. Based on the investment cost
about 1,400 ha would be financed by the Project of which 180 in Vojvodina,
220 in Croatia, 180 in Bosnia Herzegovina, 270 in Kosovo and 550 in Macedonia.
As a result of these investments, the farmer total net income would increase
from Din 33,100 to Din 73,340 at full development, five years after the
Project starts. The expected rate of return of the investment over 20 years
would be about 16%.

Farm Model No. 5: 5 ha Individual Farm with 1 ha Cherries
1.        Model No. 5 represents a 5 ha individual farm where the farmer would
shift from mainly grain and sunflower to fruit production. The model presented
includes only sour cherries but subloans would be provided also for other fruit
categories like apples, peaches, plums, pears and soft fruits. Under pre-
project condition 3.7 ha are cultivated with maize and wheat, 1.0 ha with sun-
flower and 0.3 ha under vegetables usually near to the farmhouse. The farmer
still uses animal power for land preparation but rents harvesting services
from nearby kombinats or cooperatives. The Project income would be Din
17.470 per farm.
2.        The investment would include plant material, machinery services,
fertilizer and the purchase of a 6.8-HP two wheel motor cultivator with plow
trailer, tiller and sprayer. In case that several farmers want to develop
larger units and to explore them jointly, it would be possible for 3 to 4
farmers to substitute the individually owned motor cultivators by a large
35-HP tractor with implements. Total investment cost per farm would be
Din 86,820 extended over a two year period. The financial rate of return
would be 20%. Under the Project about 900 farmers in Vojvodina, 550 in
Croatia, 650 in Bosnia Herzogovina, 900 in Macedonia and 1,820 in Serbia
would be financed.

APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Livestock Component
1.        About 90% of livestock population is concentrated in the individual
sector farms. However production share of the individual farmer sector is
relatively low because of low quality breeds, insufficient food supplies and
lack of marketing outlets (see Annex 1, paras 49-63). Demand for livestock
products has been increasing continuously during the past 15 years and is
expected to continue to increase for the next five years. The Agricultural
Development Plan and the Social Plan for 1976-1980 emphasize the need for
increased livestock productivity in the individual farmer sector where there
exists a large room for improvement. The Project would help develop production
potential of the individual farmer sector through investments totalling US$95.2
million over a three year period.
2.        The typical investments are described in 7 farm models presented
below. They include the construction of cattle stables, pig barns, sheep
barns, poultry houses, silos and slurry pits, the purchase of heifers, ewes,
rams and farm equipment including tractors and implements, silo combines and
milking machines. The farm models are:
Farm Models                  Location                      Appendix
Dairy Development - North    Vojvodina, Slovenia,
Croatia, Serbia, Kosovo              1
Dairy Development - South    Bosnia - Herzegovina
Montenegro, Macedonia                2
Calf Fattening                Vojvodina, Serbia,
Bosnia & Herzegovina                 3
Pig Fattening                 Vojvodina, Serbia                    4
Sheep Breeding                Macedonia, Serbia                    5
Lamb Fattening                Montenegro, Serbia,
Bosnia - Herzegovina                 6
Broiler Production
(Individual Farm)            Kosovo                                7
3.        The projected investment, income and cash flow statements for these
models are shown in Appendices 1 to 7.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
4.        The Project would finance about 2,450 dairy farms in all republics
and provinces, about 500 calf fattening farms in Vojvodina, Bosnia-Herzegovina,
and Serbia and about 800 pig fattening farms in Vojvodina and Serbia. Sheep
development would be financed on 1,500 farms in Bosnia-Herzegovina, Montenegro,
Macedonia and Serbia and broiler production on 1,000 farms in Kosovo.
5.        A breakdown of Project cost, number of farms, list of major invest-
ment items and volume of incremental production is presented in Table 1.
Prices, yields and other technical coefficients used in the models are shown
in Table 2. Input and output prices are based on January 1977 prices. Since
prices are assumed to change proportionally over time, model calculations are
given in constant prices. A physical contingency of 5% has been included in
the investment costs to take into account differences in subprojects design
and location. As individual farmers pay tax according to the cadastral value
of their agricultural land (Annex 1, para 34) these taxes are the same before
and after the Project. They are therefore not taken into account in the
financial analysis of the subprojects. Family labor has not been costed
because of slight underemployment among individual farmers.
6.        Because of the small size of the farms, machines such as tractors
and implements or silo combines are justified only if they are used on several
farms. This is already acheived by different means. In some cases farmers
join together in a small association to purchase and operate the machinery,
as it is often the case in Slovenia for silo combines. In other instances a
farmer buys and operates the machinery, and provides services to his neighbor-
ing farmers against payment. This is a common practice in many areas. The
models which represent the typical farm exploitation include therefore only
a share of the tractor cost. As shown in farm model 1 a farmer who will be
investing alone in a machinery equipment would be collecting enough income
from his customers to repay for his equipment. In this model a tractor is
assumed to service about 5 farmers including the owner. Total service charge
paid by the 4 farmers to the owner is expected to amount to about Din 55,000
per year, of which about Din 40,000 would contribute to repay an annual debt
installment of Din 57,400.
7.        The respective investments, financial rates of return for the pro-
posed investments as well as sensitivity tests applied to them are summarized
below:

ANNEX 4
Appendix 1
APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Livestock Component
Farm Model 1: Dairy Development - North
1.        Dairy production is the most important activity to be developed by
the Project in the individual farmer sector, and would be a major project com-
ponent in all republics or provinces. Due to different farm size and weather
conditions, two separate dairy farm models have been prepared to assess the
feasibility of possible on-farm investment. From the experience of the First
Credit Project, it appears that in most cases the farms having a good potential
for development would be the ones whose owners are fully employed in farming
rather than the smallest farms whose owners are often part-time farmers (with
off-farm income representing 70-80% of total income).
2.        The first model represents a typical farm to be developed in the
northern republics (Slovenia, Croatia, Serbia and Vojvodina). The farmer has
got already four good cows (Simmenthal breed), and would expand the herd to 10
cows by buying 4 and rearing 2 from his own herd. Investments would vary from
farm to farm, but would include building of a new barn or improvement and
extension of an existing one, silo construction, a slurry pit, a milking
machine, and farm machinery for feed production (tractor, trailer, hay rake,
mower, baker, slurry tanker, silo combine ...) usually to be shared between
several farmers (see Annex 4, para 6). Dairy cows would be either purchased
locally or imported (Simmenthal breed usually). Based on the average invest-
ment cost described below, and on the funds allocated for these developments,
the number of farms to be financed by the Project would be about 250 in
Vojvodina, 260 in Slovenia, 500 in Croatia, 330 in Serbia, and 360 in Kosovo
or a total of 1,700 farms, over a three-year period. The number of farms to
be financed under the Project represent about 10-15% of the programs designed
for the areas where the proposed Project investment would take place.
3.        The feed base would be increased by intensifying meadows and pas-
tures and by replacing some grain production by maize for silage. Grain
production would also benefit from mechanization. All crops would benefit
from the availability of slurry. Male calves, assumed to be sold at about
120 kg before Project would be intensively fed to a weight of 320 kg at
10 months.
4.        Technical coefficients, investment costs, herd projections and farm
income and expenditures statement and cash flow are presented in Tables 1
through 4 of this Appendix. The financial rates of return calculated for
the proposed investment are satisfactory. They vary between 17% and 24%
depending on revenue and cost assumptions.

ANNEX 4
Appendix 2
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Livestock Component
Farm Model 2: Dairy Development - South
1.        This second model would apply to Bosnia, Montenegro and Macedonia.
It describes the case of a farmer having three low yielding cows (Busha breed),
buying five dairy cows (improved breed such as Simmenthal or Montafon)? and
using the heifers produced on the farm to replace the Busha cows by Simmenthal.
As in Model 1, investments would include buildings, silos, farm machinery and
the purchase of animals which can be partly imported. Based on the average
investment cost estimated below, and on the allocation of funds, the number of
farms of this type to be financed by the Project would be 579 in Bosnia, 480
in Montenegro and 220 in Macedonia.
2.        All crops would be intensified by mechanization and increased use
of inputs, including on-farm produced manure. Some grain production would
be replaced by maize for silage or green fodder to increase feed production
(Appendix 2, Table 2). Assumptions used for the improved animals are the
same as in Model 1, but with a slightly lower milk yield. In year 2 and 3,
only 2.5 ha of maize fodder would be necessary. With irrigation yields
would be similar to those in Model 1 and less land would be necessary to
maintain the cattle.
3.        Herd development projections are given in Appendix 1, Table 1.
They constitute the basis for the calculation of cost estimates for invest-
ments, operating costs and revenues presented in Table 3. Financial analysis
of the model presented in Appendix 2, Table 4 shows that the returns would
vary between 15% and 21% according to revenue and cost assumptions.

ANNEX 4
Appendix 3
Page 1
APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Livestock Component
Farm Model 3: Calf Fattening
1.        Intensive beef fattening would be developed by the Project in Serbia,
Vojvodina and Bosnia-Herzegovina. Various schemes have been presented to the
mission, varying in intensity (liveweight gain 0.9 to 1.3 kg per day) and in
duration (130 days, from 120 to 400 kg liveweight, or 180 days, from 200-220
kg to 400-440 kg liveweight).
2.        Investments would include a fattening barn equipped with drinkers
and feeders (including possibly belt conveyors for feed), silos, farm machinery
for feed production. A farm unit would have a capacity varying from 20 to 100
calves - farmers could also be grouped (say 5 together) to develop larger units
(500 calves per cycle).
3.        The model presented below describes a typical case of a farmer
shifting from grain production to maize fodder (green or silage) for meat
production. Concentrate feed, feeder animals, veterinary services, technical
advice and market outlet would be provided by a kombinat or a cooperative.
Because of the volatility of the beef export meat (Annex 1, para 50) each
subproject would have to present detailed information on the expected export
markets and would have to be reviewed and approved by the World Bank.
4.        There would be two cycles of 6 month fattening, from an initial
weight of 220 kg to a final weight of 440 kg (liveweight gain = 1.22 kg/day).
The feed would be fodder maize produced by the farmer, used either as green
or as silage, depending on the season, and a concentrate (maize grain, bran
soybean meal, minerals). Average intake would be 17 kg of fodder or silage
and 3 kg of concentrate per day.
5.        Based on the investment cost of the typical farm presented in the
model and on the allocation of funds for this activity, the number of farms
to be financed by the Project would be 170 in Serbia, 165 in Vojvodina and
165 in Bosnia-Herzegovina.
6.        Financial analysis of this model presented in Appendix 3, Table 3
shows the extreme sensitivity of the investment to the market price of meat.
The investment is however justified in view of its normally high return (27%)
and the relative unlikely event of drastic drop in market prices in the future.

YUGOSLAVIA
Livestock Component
Farm Model 4: Pig Fattening
1.        Pig production would be developed by the Project in Serbia and
Vojvodina. The model presented here includes only investments in facilities
for pig fattening, but the Project could also finance facilities for sows.
2.        The typical farmer would have a 6 ha farm, including 4 ha of maize,
or other cereals, I ha of industrial crop (sugarbeet, sunflower), and I ha
for the house farm with garden and orchard, representing a gross income of
about Din 90,000 and a net income of Din 50,000. Although not included in
the model for which changes in cropping pattern are not expected under the
Project, investments for farm mechanization could be financed by the Project
if justified for an increase of grain production.
3.        Pig production will be developed in areas where it is already
well-known activity and where good performance can be expected without diffi-
culties. The capacity of the stable would be for about 100 pigs. Fattening
operations would involve three production cycles per year to be delivered to
a kombinat or a cooperative which would supply the farmers with piglets and
mixed feed on credit in kind, and provide him with technical assistance and
veterinary services. The number of farmers involved would be about 350 in
Vojvodina and 450 in Serbia. Estimates for investment costs, operating ex-
penses and revenues are given in Appendix 4, Table 1 and financial analysis
of the Model is presented in Appendix 4, Table 2. As for calf fattening,
the investment is sensitive to feed cost and to the price of pork, however
in view of the high financial return under prevailing circumstances (37%)
and high demand for pork in Yugoslavia, the investment is justified.

Livestock Component
Farm Model 5: Sheep Breeding
1.        This model describes the development of a flock by expanding its
size and building new wintering barn facilities and by improving the feed
supply through pasture improvement. The introduction of good quality ewes
and rams - partly imported - would also improve the breed. This scheme
would apply to about 650 farms in Macedonia, and about 150 farms in Serbia.
2.        By expanding the ewes number from 100 to 160 - both from purchases
and by keeping female lambs - and by improving flock productivity, it is
expected that farmers net income would increase by about 50% in four years
(20% after debt service).
3.        The basic data for the cost-benefit calculations i.e. the flock
development model, income and expenditure statement and investment costs
are presented in Appendix 5, Tables 1, 2 and 3. Financial analysis of the
model is presented in Table 4 and shows an adequate financial rate of return.

A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Livestock Component
Farm Model 6: Lamb Fattening
1.        Lamb fattening would be developed by the Project in three republics:
Bosnia-Herzegovina, Montenegro and Serbia. Various schemes have been pres-
ented to the mission, including simple complementary feeding or more intensive
use of concentrate feed, and involving one, two or three cycles per year.
There are also proposals to group a few farmers together to save on buildings
and labor and increase the return.
2.        The model presented here describes only one of the possible types of
lamb fattening, to show how this production can be profitable. It describes
the case of a farmer having a breeding flock of 100 ewes and undertaking lamb
fattening operation simultaneously. The investment would include construc-
tion of a new barn to be used for lamb fattening in spring, summer and autumn
(2 cycles per year) and for the ewes in winter, provision of miscellaneous
machinery for hay making and transport, feed mixing equipment and in some
cases construction of a silo for feed storage. Investment costs are given
in Appendix 6, Table 1. The number of farmers financed would be about 350
in Bosnia-Herzegovina, 200 in Montenegro and 150 in Serbia.
3.        The pre-Project situation is the one described for the Year 0 in the
Farm Model 5 (Sheep Breeding).  Fattening would be introduced in two cycles:
1st Cycle:  2 month fattening of 150 lambs born in February and
sold in May at 25 kg liveweight. Fifty lambs come from the
farmers flock and 100 lambs are bought at 15 kg.
2nd Cycle:  2 month fattening of 100 yearlings bought at 25 kg
liveweight and sold at 35 kg and of 20 culled ewes from the
farmer's flock, sold at 45 kg. Short term credit to buy animals
and feed would be provided by kombinats or "Zadrugas" (cooperatives)
having contract with the farmers. These kombinats would supply
concentrate feed and buy the finished animals.
4.        Financial analysis of the model is in Appendix 6, Table 3.  It in-
dicates a good return under prevailing cost and benefit conditions. As for
calf and pig fattening, the investment is sensitive to the price of meat.
In view, however, of the high demand for lamb in the domestic market, the
price of lamb is likely to remain high and maintain durably the prevailing
cost benefit relationship.



ANNEX 4

YUGOSLAVIA
Livestock Component
Farm Model 6: Individual Farmer Sector Broiler Production
1.        The scheme would apply to about 1,000 farms in Kosovo as part of
a full programa including a slaughterhouse and a farm with hatchery in the
social sector (Annex 5). The suggested capactiy is 750 broilers per batch,
or 3,000 broilers per year (4 cycles per year). Housing will be of the deep
litter type with adequate provision for ventilation and heating.
2.        Farmers would keep the broilers for about nine weeks until they
reach about 1.5 kg liveweight. Counting for the clean-out between each batch,
it is estimated that an annual output of four batches would be developed. The
stocking density estimated at 10 broilers per m2, could be increased to 12,
and the number of batches to 4.5 or even more with an effective management,
thus increasing further the return per building. The kombinat would provide
on credit the day old chicks together with the suitable types of mixed feed
(starter - finisher). The kombinat would in return purchase the broilers at
an agreed date for processing in Project financed facilities.
3.        Financial analysis of the investment shown in Appendix 7, Table 2
indicates a good return on investment at prevailing prices (28%).

APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
Agroindustries
Agroindustrial Development in Yugoslavia
1.        In general agroindustrial development in Yugoslavia is lagging.
The processing industry has not kept pace with the increase in agricultural
production and therefore cannot provide the variety and quantity of processed
products that the growing affluence of the population can now afford and
demand. Ultimately, it does not exploit fully the potential of the export
markets to the West European countries particularly the German Federal Republic.
In broad terms the existing processing industry is to a large extent insuffi-
cient, limited in product spectrum, and to some extent obsolescent.
2.        The Federal Government and the government of each Republic or Prov-
ince are aware of this problem and in collaboration with the agroindustrial
kombinats have formulated plans for important expansions and modernizations of
the agroindustrial sector. In accordance with investments underway and planned
for increasing agricultural production and to maintain an adequate correlation
between production and processing capacity, the Government has placed priority
on developing the processing industry for milk, for meat, and for fruits and
vegetables.
Milk Processing
3.        Background.  The emphasis on milk production is clearly reflected in
the following statistics on the total production of milk in Yugoslavia, includ-
ing cow and sheep production.
Milk Production (Lts. Million)
1960                       2434
1965                       2400
1970                       2655
1971                       2650
1972                       2879
1973                       3159
1974                       3531
1975                       3688
The marked increase in milk production is mainly due to the introduction of
high yielding dairy breeds: Simmenthal in the North and Holstein in the South,
mostly for dairy farms in the social sector. In fact the social sector accounts
for over 30% of the milk production in the country. Yugoslavia is still a net
importer of fresh milk which is acquired mostly from Bulgaria; in 1970 the
demand for milk and milk products was equivalent to 75 lts. per capita annually,



ANNEX 5
Page 2
however, this is expected to increase to 130 lts. in 1980. The largest poten-
tial for increased production lies in the individual farmer sector, however,
the main constraints are the low yielding cows (Busha breed) that predominates
in this sector, producing only about 1,000/1,200 lts per lactation and the lack
of organized milk collection from the numerous small farmers. In 1972, only
about 15% of the milk produced went to industrial processing. Milk prices
to the producer avterage about Din. 3.40/lt of cow's milk with about 3.6% of
butter-fat. This includes a premium of about Din 0.5/liter paid both by the
Federal and the Government of each Republic. The price is comparatively high
by world standards (US 0.19/lt) and enables the farmer to purchase feed and
concentrate which is available at about Din. 2.40/kg. Artificial insemination
is also generally available to the private farmers through the extension
services of the Ministry of Agriculture.
4.        Project Features.  Within the described framework and Government's
policy of placing all agroindustries in the social sector the approach to in-
creased milk collection from the individual farmers has been through the es-
tablishment of village milk collection centers owned and maintained by the
kombinats. The centers comprise essentially a milk chilling tank with mechani-
cal refrigeration placed in a room in the house of a producer who, for a fee
from the kombinat, has agreed to provide the space, operate the unit and record
the milk deliveries from the contributing producers. To place the centers
within reasonable reach of milk producers, there would be at least one center
in each village. The chilling tank would have a capacity of about 500 liters
considering the small surplus offered for sale by each farmer. Milk tank-trucks
would collect the milk daily for delivering to the kombinat processing plants.
5.        In addition to milk collection facilities and milk tank trucks the
project would finance a corresponding expansion of some of the existing dairy
processing plants, including modernization, and provision of equipment for pro-
duction of a greater spectrum of dairy products.
6.        In summary the following subprojects related to Milk Processing would
be financed:

7.        In the interest of effecting economies of scale, kombinats within
the same Republic, coordinate their investments in a manner that will lead
each kombinat to specialize in one particular form of processing. With this
approach one kombinat would devote exclusively its milk processing activities
to cheese making while another kombinat, in the same Republic, would confine
itself to production of fluid pasturized milk and so on. This policy would
reduce the flexibility of each plant to adapt to seasonal variations in supply
and, equally important, would eliminate competition. The mission advised
strongly against the adoption of this policy. Although milk prices are con-
trolled in Yugoslavia, milk processing is an economically viable activity and
financing of milk processing related projects appear justified as suggested
by the bypothetical model described on tables 3, 4 and 5, showing a financial
rate of return of 16%.



ANNEX 5
Page 4
Meat Processing
8.        Background.  Yugoslavia's cattle population declined in the late
sixties. However, since 1971 there has been a gradual recovery in this sector
which is over 90% in private hands.
Cattle (Head '000)
Total           Total           %
Population    Slaughtered    Offtake
1960                         5709             --           --
1965
1970
1971                         5138             1954         38%
1972                         5148            1718          33%
1973                         5366            1886          35%
1974                         5681            2155          38%
1975                         5872            2490          42%
9.        The high-offtake results from the fact that nearly 50% of the stock
slaughtered are males up to six months of age. Close to 40% of the cattle are
Simmenthal breed, the balance, a mixture of indigenous breeds with predominance
of the Bosnian Busha type. Yugoslavia shows a trend toward increasing the pro-
portion of dairy cattle (dual purpose) at the expense of strictly beef cattle,
however, lack of fooder has been the main constraint to livestock growth in
general. Funds being channelled into irrigation and the individual farmer sec-
tor are expected to result in increased production of fodder and agricultural
by-products for breeding dairy cows and fattening males. The financial incen-
tives for this are expected to come from increased demand of milk, dairy
products, and also meat and processed meat products (Annex 1, para 49-63).
10.       The meat processing industry which is centered mostly in Serbia
with about 50% of Yugoslavia's total capacity and in Croatia with about 25%,
developed during the late sixties when exports of fresh meats, mostly to the
U.K. reached close to 25% of the total production. Exports of fresh meats have
since declined and the main markets are currently Italy, Greece, Switzerland
and Sweden. Yugoslavia is also an important exporter of live cattle, however,
exports, mostly to Italy, Greece, Lybia and Tunisia have also dropped during
the recent years. The processing industry now mostly composed of plants built
to standards of 10-15 years ago faces need for expansion and modernization to
meet export standards as well as the increased domestic demand.
11.       Project Features.  In view of the scope of the renovation program of
the Serbian and Croatian meat plants, government has decided to cover that
program separately, consequently the Project would only focus on the meat pro-
cessing industry in the Republics of Bosnia-Herzegovina and Montenegro as
shown below:

Meat Processing Plants

Expansion/Modernization of Existing

Bosnia-Herzegovina                                1         500 tons/yr
12.       Domestic meat prices are controlled, however, not so the prices of
processed meats. Processing plants selling both fresh and processed meats are
financially viable operations as suggested by the hypothetical model described
on Tables 15, 16 and 17. The financial rate of return is shown as 19%.
13.       To meet the projected demand for poultry meat, the Project would also
finance a broiler producing and slaughtering complex of 2.5 million birds a
year for the Republic of Kosovo. The facility would include parent stock and
its own production of chicks, 1.5 million day-old chicks would be sold to the
individual farmer sector (about 1,000 farmers) and one million would be reared
in the complex. The slaughtering plant would have a capacity of 2.5 million
birds yearly including thus the 1.5 million finished broilers that would be
bought back from the individual farmers. Details of the Project are shown
on Tables 12, 13 and 14. This subproject appears economically viable. Its
estimated financial rate of return would be 20.0%.
Fruits and Vegetables
14.       Background.  This sector has also registered important gains in
most crops during the last decade, reflected by the following statistics:
Vegetables

15.       The objectives of the processing industry in this sector is not only
to cope with the increased production but to maximize the utilization of the
fresh produce by cold storage, grading, processing and reducing spoilage.
Yugoslavia is notoriously short of cold store facilities in the fruit growing
regions in Serbia. Facilities are not sufficient for adequately grading apples
and pears and thereby allow a legitimate premium on the high quality fruit and
a correspondingly lower price on the grades destined to processing. In general
the prices commanded by the Yugoslavian processed fruits and vegetables in the
export markets of Western Europe are lower than the domestic prices and in some
cases by as much as 20%. To promote exports the Federal Governments offers a
premium payment to the processors. In the case of pickled peppers and other
vegetables the premium amounts to 17% on the selling price.
16.       With adequate grading facilities fruits and vegetables can be placed
in their proper price ranges and processing can become more competitive in the
export market. Western Europen countries and particularly the German Federal
Republic are large importers of brine-pickled vegetables, frozen vegetables,
processed tomato, dehydrated vegetables and apple and pear juices. The
statistics below show present and projected demand of these products by the
Western European countries.
17.       Prccessed vegetables.  In spite of strong competition from frozen
and dehydrated products, the consumption of canned and bottled vegetables
has tended to increase and is expected to grow in the foreseeable future.

19.       Frozen vegetables.  Trade in frozen vegetables in Yugoslavia is
limited by inadequate storage and transport facilities. Shop storage in

particular is lacking, and retail sales are almost entirely restricted to
self-service food stores. Frozen vegetable consumption is therefore very
low in Yugoslavia, it is estimated at between 1,000 and 2,000 tons/year. How-
ever by 1980 this could well reach about 5,000 tons/year.  Exports markets
offer great potential however.

The European trade in frozen vegetables has been increasing at a fast rate.
Total annual imports doubled between 1965 to 1970. Most of the industrial
nations in Western Europe are expected to offer market potential for exports
of Yugoslavian frozen vegetables. The frozen vegetables consumed in Western
Europe are mostly peas, spinach, beans, brussel sprouts, however other types
such as carrots, leeks, peppers, sweetcorn, mushroom and asparagus are be-
coming increasingly popular, particularly in the German Federal Republic.

22.       Processed fruits.  The total production of apples is predicted to
reach 450,000 tons in 1980. Exports of fresh apples have been insignificant
reaching only 9,500 tons in 1972, however the market for processed apples
and pear juice is good, with the German Federal Republic appearing as the
main potential importer. Relevant statistics are as follow:

23.       Project Features.  In view of the foregoing the project would finance
the following subprojects related to processing of fruits and vegetables.

The cold stores and complementary grading and packing plants proposed for
Serbia are described in detail on Tables 9, 10 and 11. They are considered
economically viable with a financial rate of return of 27%. A hypothetical
model of a representative fruit and vegetable processing plant is described
on Tables 6, 7 and 8. The model shows a financial rate of return of 14% and
serves to support recommended financing in these type of plants.
24.       Although strictly not a fruit processing plant, the Project would
also support the construction of a winery for the Republic of Macedonia. The
capacity would be for crushing 600 tons of wine grapes a day and holding up
to 10 million liters of wine. The winery would, probably be owned by the
Agroindustrial Kombinat OVCE POLE in Sveti Nikole, it would not have facili-
ties for stabilizing and bottling wine nor for processing of marc and lees
which is already provided by plants under construction under the Bank's First
Agroindustries Project for Macedonia (Loan 894). The winery model is de-
scribed in detail on Tables 18, 19 and 20. The winery would serve to process
the increased production of wine grapes in Macedonia and also replace several
small obsolete and unhygienic wine fermenting facilities in the individual
sector. The sub-project is economically viable showing a financial rate
of return of 29.0%.
Grain Driers
25.       The Project would finance about 7 grain drying facilities for the
Republic of Vojvodina. The units should be direct fired by gas or indirect
fired with light grade fuel oil however, not with the intermediate production



ANNEX 5
Page 11
of steam for heating the air which would be uneconomical. The capacity of
each unit would be about 30 tons per hour assuming a moisture reduction in the
grain from up to 40% down to 14%. Table 2 shows investment details pertaining
to this subproject.

Operating  Costs + 10b   : 22%
Revenue             - 10%      18%

including engineering, duties and physical contingencies.
6/ 7% after interest charges and Din 4.9 million depreciation charge.

ANNEX 9

APPRAISAL OF
A SECOND AGRICULTURAL CREDIT PROJECT
YUGOSLAVIA
Economic Rate of Return of the Project
1.       sssssTThe economic rate of return was calculated on the basis of quanti-
fiable economic costs and benefits generated. Costs and benefits are based
on the representative investment models as described in Annexes 3, 4 and 5,
and were evaluated at shadow prices as discussed below. Training and admin-
istrative expenses were also included in the costs. Results derived from
the analysis are considered conservative since some benefits attributable
to the Project, such as improved resource allocation in the agricultural and
agroindustries sectors because of training and better sub-project appraisal
and supervision, and health benefits to consumers of hygienically processed
food, were deemed non-quantifiable and, therefore, were excluded from the
economic rate of return determination.
2.        The economic rate of return calculations are based on the following
assumptions:
(a) Output Prices. For wheat, maize, wool and tobacco the
Bank's commodity world price projections (in constant 1976
prices) were used as basic data. The development of the
beef prices was assumed to be in accordance with the Bank's
beef price projections (in constant 1976 prices). The
price of milk was reduced by the subsidiy of Din 0.5/liter.
The remaining products have been priced at the prevailing
farmgate prices;
(b) Input Prices. Investment items have been priced at constant
end 1976 prices less import duties levied on import compo-
nents. Prices of feed grains have been projected on the
basis of the development of the Bank's commodity world price
estimates. Prices of replacement cattle have been projected
to change according to the beef price. Other inputs have
been valued at end 1976 farmgate prices, adjusted by taxes
where applicable;
(c) Labor. Hired labor has been valued at the prevailing wage
rates. For investments family labor has been accounted for
at the wage rate for unskilled labor. Family labor has not
been costed as operating expenses because there are unused
labor capacities on private farmers at the pre-Project stage.
It is estimated that about 280 mandays family labor are avail-
able in a typical individual farm of which about 80-100 man-
days are in excess and would not have an opportunity to be
employed without project investments;



ANNEX 9
Page 2
(d)  Processing Investments.  Benefits were calculated on the
basis of value added through processing. Benefits due to
reduced losses were added. Calculation of benefits from cold
storage investments has been based on the price difference of
products sold at harvest and off season time and reduction
of losses;
(e) Administrative and Training Costs. Costs have been based
on salaries of additional staff to be hired by the Bank of
Vojvodina and the Participating Banks;
(f) Exchange Rate. The prevailing exchange rate of Din 18 per
US$1 has been used for all calculations.
3.        Based on these assumptions, the Project benefits and costs have
been calculated as shown in the attached table. The overall Project's eco-
nomic rate of return would be about 22%.
The sensitivity analysis shows the following results:
Investment Costs           +10%              20%
Operating Costs            +10%              15%
Benefits                   -10%              12%
4.        The economic rate of return for individual component for fruit and
vegetable, livestock development and agro-industries components are 25%, 23%
and 18% respectively. Sensitivity analysis applied to the individual benefit
and costs streams of these respective components show that they remain viable
(Tables 2, 3 and 4).
5.        The overall economic rate of return indicates that the Project
would result in substantial gains to the economy.
